2.2.3 Magneto-Archimedes Separation
The stable levitation position in magneto-Archimedes levitation is
determined by the ratio of the difference of the magnetic susceptibilities to
that of the densities between the object material and its surroundings.
Therefore, different substances levitated in the field have different
equiUbrium positions. Even if some materials are levitated accidentally in
the same position under certain conditions, it is possible to change the
stable point and to separate the materials by changing the properties of the
surroundings. This appears to be a novel and useful way to separate
materials. The potential of this magneto-Archimedes separation was
evaluated through the following experiments.
Separation of colored glass particles in a solution of manganese
chloride was carried out to demonstrate the magneto-Archimedes
separation.^^ The glass is usually colored by doping with impurities.
Therefore, glasses of different colors should be different in density and in
magnetic susceptibility before undergoing magneto-Archimedes
separation. In our experiment, glass particles of four different colors, red,
blue, yellow and black, were used (Satake Glass Co., Ltd.). The densities
and magnetic susceptibilities were measured using a pycnometer and a
SQUID (Quantum Design, MPMS-5), respectively, and the following
results were obtained: red: x = -13.3 x 10 ^ p = 2.546 x 10^ kg m'\
yellow: x = -9.27 x 10"^, p = 3.208 x 10\ blue: x = -2.20 x 10"^, p =
3.171 X 10\ and black: ;t: = -h317 x 10"^, p = 3.199 x lOl Glass fragments
were passed through a sieve with 1 x 1 mm'^ mesh in advance, and the
smaller particles were used. Each of the glass particle aggregates of about
0.2 grams were put into a cylinder filled with a 6.0 wt% MnCb aqueous
solution (jf = +81.4 X 10"^, p = 1.052 x 10^), then put in an ultrasonic bath
for about one minute to eliminate air bubbles adhering onto the particle
surface. The cylinder was set so the bottom was at the maximum point of
IB ' dB/dz I. Increasing the field, the behavior of the colored glass particles
was observed.

--- Page 80 ---

60 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
(a) (b)
J
I
Fig. 2-2-4 Magneto-Archimedes separation of colored glass particles: (a) under zero magnetic field,
(b) under 8.0 T at the center of the field, (c) 9.5 T, (d) 12.0 T.
[Reproduced from N. Hirota, RIKENRev., 44, 159(2002)]
Figure 2-2-4 shows photographs of magneto-Archimedes separation
of colored glass particles taken under several magnetic fields. At the
beginning, under zero magnetic field, all the colored glass particles are at
the bottom of the cylinder, as shown in Fig. 2-2-4(a). With increase in the
magnetic field, the glass particles gradually floated in the solution. When
the magnetic field in the center reached excess of 8 T, the red particles
floated (Fig. 2-2-4(b)). Then, the yellow and blue particles floated in
succession between 9.0 to 9.5 T (Fig. 2-2-4(c)). Even under 12 T, the black
glass particles did not levitate due to their large paramagnetic
susceptibility. The value of B - dB/dz needed to levitate red, yellow and
blue particles were 205, 298 and 325 T' m~', respectively. These values
were in good agreement with the calculated ones. Comparing Figs. 2-2-4
(c) and (d), the distance between the groups of different colored glass
differed, i.e., the distance was larger in Fig. 2-2-4(c) than in (d). This is
due to the difference in the spatial distribution of B • dB/dz. Around the
maximum point, B • dB/dz varies somewhat less than in the higher region in
the z direction. Thus the resolution of magneto-Archimedes separation
becomes high in this area.

--- Page 81 ---

2.2 Magneto-Archimedes Effects and Related Effects 61
In this experiment, the order of the required absolute value of B •
dB/dz corresponds to that of the magnetic susceptibilities. Judging from
this result, the equilibrium position is determined mainly by the magnetic
susceptibility in this case. In some other experiments, such as the
separation of a KCl and NaCl powder mixture,'^^ the equilibrium position
results mainly from the difference in density rather than in magnetic
susceptibility. In magneto-Archimedes separation, one can choose which
factor should be emphasized, the difference in magnetic susceptibility or
the difference in density, by selecting the appropriate surroundings.
The colored glasses used in the above experiment were made for arts
and crafts use. To add clear colors, they contained more impurities than
glass for practical use. This results in relatively large differences in
magnetic susceptibilities and densities among samples, making magneto-
Archimedes separation easy. It would be useful if glass for practical use,
such as bottles, tableware and glass windows, could be separated
according to differences in color by magneto-Archimedes separation,
because separation by color is required for recycling. We prepared several
kinds of glassware used in daily life to determine whether separation by
differences in color was possible by magneto-Archimedes separation.
Almost all the sample glasses used in this experiment have similar
densities. However, due to the slight difference in their magnetic
susceptibilities, it was confirmed that the separation of glass by color was
possible by magneto-Archimedes separation. Magneto-Archimedes
separation may be beneficial for materials recycling.
So far, differences in magnetic properties have not been utilized for
the separation of biological materials, but it is a potential application of
magneto-Archimedes separation. This possibility has been confirmed
experimentally.'^^ Biological materials used in the study were purified
hemoglobin, fibrinogen, albumin, cholesterol and deoxyribonucleic acid
(extracted from salmon sperm). Indeed, due to the slight differences in
magnetic susceptibilities and densities, they were separated by magneto-
Archimedes technique.
There are various magnetic separation techniques, such as a method
for picking out iron materials from metal scrap using a magnet, attraction
of tiny magnetic particles onto filter wires made of steel (HGMS: high
gradient magnetic separation) and others. These techniques are based on
whether the substance is attracted or non-attracted by magnetic forces. It is
therefore impossible to separate a mixture of several substances into each
component separately. One other conventional magnetic separation
method, gravity concentration, has been well known for decades, but this
separation method is controlled only by the density of solid materials.
Therefore, those with the same density cannot be separated.
The magnetic separation of feeble magnetic particles described here
can be readily achieved with the aid of the magneto-Archimedes principle

--- Page 82 ---

62 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
using an ordinary superconducting magnet. This separation technique
results from differences in density and magnetic susceptibility of the
materials and should enable one to separate a powder mixture composed of
more than one kind of substance quickly into separate clusters. This
method is sensitive to both the density and the magnetic susceptibility of
the materials. Magneto-Archimedes separation may be a useful method for
the separation of a wide range of materials and for recycling processes.
2.2.4 Magnetic Field Effects on Some Physicochemical Processes
Sometimes, unexpected effects are caused by magnetic force in some
physicochemical processes. In this section, such magnetic force effects on
physicochemical processes are introduced.
A. Magnetic Field Effect on Oxygen Dissolution Process in Water'^^
Gas dissolution into water and other liquids is an elemental step in
numerous processes. The oxygen dissolution process plays an important
role especially in the respiration of living bodies and in chemical and
metallurgical processes such as corrosion. Many studies have reported that
the concentration of dissolved oxygen in water is enriched by magnetic
fields.^^^ Most of them report an increment of the amount of dissolved
oxygen by up to 50% under less than 1 T of field. However,
thermodynamical considerations indicate that it is impossible to increase
the equilibrium amount of dissolved oxygen to such a high degree. The
calculated increment of the equilibrium oxygen concentration is 7 ppm in
the case of 1 T and 0.04% even under a high magnetic field of 8 T.
Therefore, it was a matter of interest for us to examine whether the
concentration of oxygen is in any way influenced under a magnetic field.
The experiments were carried out as follows. Seven cubic
centimeters of distilled water was introduced into the glass sample
container (diameter: 20 mm; length: 70 mm). Then the water was
degassed. The temperature around the sample space was regulated with
circulating water to 15.0 ± O.TC. The magnetic field was applied on the
sample container by a cryocooler-operated superconducting magnet with
an open room-temperature bore. The field homogeneity along the length of
the sample container (70 mm) was within 5.5%. Oxygen gas was then
introduced into the sample space at a pressure of 5 x 10*^ Pa.
After each prescribed duration of time, the water container was
disconnected from the system and the water was transferred into a small
test tube. An electrochemical oxygen sensor (TOA Electronics Ltd., Model
DO-25) was used to measure the dissolved oxygen concentration. The
change in the pressure of oxygen in the gas phase during the dissolution
was estimated to be below 0.2% considering the equilibrium amount of
oxygen dissolution into the sample water. Under the field distribution of
the superconducting magnet, the change in the surface area due to the

--- Page 83 ---

2.2 Magneto-Archimedes Effects and Related Effects 63
Moses effect was estimated to be about 0.03% for a field intensity of 4 T.
The zero-field experiment was performed following the exact same
procedure as described above except that the magnet was switched off.
First, it was examined whether or not the equilibrium value of
dissolved oxygen concentration in water was affected by the field. To attain
a sufficient equilibrium state, the sample water was exposed to oxygen for
12 hours under 0 T and 4 T The amount of oxygen dissolved under 0 T
was 21.44 g m"^ on average with a standard deviation of 0.70 g m"\ Under
4 T, it was 21.90 g m~^ on average with a standard deviation of 0.61 g m"\
The difference between the two sets of results was smaller than the
standard deviation, which was about 3%. Therefore, it can be concluded
that the equilibrium concentration of dissolved oxygen in water does not
differ by more than three percent when a field of 4 T is applied.
Next, the concentration of dissolved oxygen after one hour of
exposure of water to oxygen was measured. The amount of dissolved
oxygen was larger under 4 T than that under zero field. The average amount
of oxygen and the standard deviation were 12.89 g m'^ and 0.50 g m"^
under 0 T and 17.67 g m"^ and 0.41 g m'"^ under 4 T, respectively. The
observed enhancement by the field was as large as 37% under 4 T These
two observations after 12 hours and 1 hour of exposure time indicate that
the dissolution rate of oxygen into water was increased by the field.
To confirm the above indication of change in the oxygen dissolution
kinetics under different fields, the time dependence of the oxygen
dissolution into water was examined under 0, 2 and 4 T. Results are shown
in Fig. 2-2-5. It is noted that the final concentraUon nearly reaches the
same equilibrium value under every one of the three different fields.
However, in the shorter time range required to attain equilibrium described
herein, it was clear that the dissolution of oxygen was accelerated by the
presence of the magnetic fields. The above results show that the kinetics of
oxygen dissolution into water is enhanced significantly by the application
of magnefic fields, while the equilibrium of oxygen dissolution remains
unchanged under the fields.
Concerning the possible mechanism of the kinetic enhancement of
oxygen dissolution into water by magnetic fields, there are three essential
steps involved in the dissolution process.
(a) Supply of oxygen to the gas-water interface from the gas phase.
(b) Oxygen dissolution at the gas-water interface.
(c) Movement of dissolved oxygen molecules into the bulk of the water
phase.
First, pure oxygen gas was employed in the present experiment to
provide constant pressure during the dissolution process and hence, the gas
phase was dominantly oxygen, considering the equilibrium water vapor
pressure below 2 x 10^ Pa. Since the diffusion in the gas phase is quite
fast, there should not be any significant gradient of oxygen partial pressure

--- Page 84 ---

[Reproduced from Y. Ikezoe et al., / Magn. Soc. Jpn., 22, 821(1998)]
in the gas phase, which makes step (a) unlikely as the rate-determining
step.
To accelerate the dissolution reaction of oxygen at the water surface,
step (b), either the activation energy of the reaction should be reduced or
the frequency factor of the reaction should be raised by the magnetic field.
From the thermodynamic point of view, it is not likely that the activation
energy is altered by the fields because the magnetic energy given to
oxygen gas is sufficiently small, i.e., 0.27 J mol"' under 4 T.
If step (c) is the rate-determining step, the kinetics should depend on
the diffusion process of the oxygen molecule in water in the absence of
convection of the liquid phase. From the dissolution curve in Fig. 2-2-5,
the diffusion coefficient can be estimated assuming a diffusion-controlled
process from the water surface into the bulk and a constant surface
concentration of oxygen equal to the equilibrium value. The diffusion
coefficient of oxygen in water thus obtained was 7.17 x 10"^ m^ s~\ 14.2 x
10"^ m^ s-\ and 19.6 x 10"^ m^ s' under 0, 2 and 4 T, respectively,
corresponding to the reduction of the activation energy of diffusion by
1.64 kJ mol"^ and 2.41 kJ mor\ respectively. Such a large enhancement of
oxygen diffusion by the field seems to be unlikely due to the smallness of
the magnetic energy.
Another possibility in this category is the enhancement of oxygen
movement in water by the magnetically induced convection of water. In
the dissolution process, a gradient of oxygen concentration may be formed
in water, i.e., dissolved oxygen is denser near the water surface than
around the bottom of the glass vessel. This means that a gradient of
volume magnetic susceptibility is created in water due to the paramagnetic

--- Page 85 ---

2.2 Magneto-Archimedes Effects and Related Effects 65
nature of oxygen while water is diamagnetic. Under a non-uniform
magnetic field, the convection may be induced by the non-uniformity of
the magnetic force acting on the water. By such a magnetically induced
convection, water is stirred, and the oxygen dissolution is accelerated.
Actually, the space used in previous experiments around the magnetic field
center has 5.5% field inhomogeneity.
An analysis was made based on hydrodynamics analogous to thermal
convection. Water can be treated as an incompressible fluid, and the
equation of continuity then becomes

--- Page 86 ---

66 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
According to this convection mechanism, the degree of acceleration
should depend on the amplitude of the product of the magnetic field and its
gradient, B • dB/dz, and the magnetic susceptibility of gas. These features
were confirmed experimentally.'^^ All the observations support the
mechanism that the acceleration of oxygen dissolution into water is caused
by magnetically induced convection due to the non-uniformity of magnetic
susceptibility and the magnetic field in water.
The observations above appear to have great significance. When
biological processes in nature are considered, the metabolism of living
creatures depends on oxygen dissolution into water. The above results
suggest that certain biological processes that are subjected to magnetic
fields in the range of a few to several teslas may be affected via a change
in the dissolution kinetics of oxygen into water. Moreover, since the
convection of liquid plays an important role in some chemical and
metallurgical processes, processes that include oxygen dissolution or
consumption should be affected by magnetic fields due to the magnetically
induced convection.
B. Magnetic Field Effect on Water Vaporization'^^
In the course of studies on microbial processes, faster drying of incubation
solutions under fields was sometimes noticed. In order to confirm the
observation quantitatively, a simplified experiment was designed to observe
the rate of vaporization of water precisely in the presence of fields. Under
flowing dry carrier gases, nitrogen, air and oxygen, on the water surface,
the water vaporization rate was monitored by a humidity sensor placed in
the outlet gas. First, no magnetic influence was observed in the case of
nitrogen, while significant field effect was observed for air and oxygen.
Examination of how the magnetic field is related to the enhancement
led us to propose the following mechanism, which is similar to the
mechanism of the magnetic field acceleration of oxygen dissolution into
water. The paramagnetic volume susceptibility of air, Xair = +0.3736 x 10"^,
is mainly contributed by oxygen gas, ;fo:= +1.8028 x 10"^, while nitrogen
and water vapors are both diamagnetic with much smaller contributions,
XN,= -0.0063 X 10-^ and ;fH:0 = -0.0068 x 10~^ at 293 K, 101 kPa. During
the vaporization process, a susceptibility gradient could be created along
the vertical direction in the atmosphere above the water surface. This is
because the water vapor content should be larger near the water surface
than at a distance if the diffusion process of water vapor in the atmosphere
is the rate-determining step of the vaporization. The saturated vapor
pressure of water, 2338 Pa, in air at 293 K reduces the susceptibility of the
air 2.35% less than the ;t'air of the dry air. The difference in the volume
magnetic force exerted on the wet air near the water surface and the dry air
in bulk would be 2.2 N m"\ This difference would be as large as the
driving force expected when thermal convection takes place between two

--- Page 87 ---

2.2 Magneto-Archimedes Effects and Related Effects 67
air regions of different temperatures, 293 and 293 + 50 K. Thus, a rather
strong magnetic convection induced by water vaporization should be
expected. This hypothesis was confirmed experimentally as reported
elsewhere. ^^^
As a consequence, when living organisms are placed under a
magnetic field with gradient, they may feel colder due to the latent heat of
vaporization, and they may experience aqueous environments with
different concentrations of ingredients from the intended values. These
changes in environmental conditions should necessarily cause some
change in the experimental results when living organisms are subjected to
the magnetic field for examination. Therefore, special consideration must
be taken when determining whether the field really has an effect on living
organisms.
C. Magnetic Field Control of Thermal Convection
On the basis of the preceding findings, we devised a wind tunnel that can
generate wind through a tube placed in a magnet but without any moving
mechanical parts in it.^^^ This is based on the following generalized
observations. The volume magnetic susceptibility of the heated air
decreases with T as 1/rl The neighboring colder air in the tube tends to
replace the heated air, pushing it out of the high field region in order to
maximize the total magnetization of the air, thereby minimizing the
magnetic energy. The procedure was based on the imbalance of the
magnetic force made by asymmetrical heating.
In the vertical direction, the heating air generates thermal convection,
i.e., an upward flow in the heater tube. By applying a high and steep
gradient magnetic field in such a system, it is possible to control the
thermal convection.'^^ Based on the above-mentioned mechanism, it seems
possible to accelerate the upward thermal convection by applying a field
below the heating region. On the other hand, reversal of the upward flow
may occur when a field is applied above the heating region. Indeed, it was
ascertained that a magnetic field is applicable for controlling thermal
convection. The use of the field can even reverse the direction of the
thermal convection of the air in a vertical furnace. The magnetically
induced airflow described in this section is fairly large in volume and has
no mechanically driven parts. Therefore, by using this technique, it is
possible to control thermal convection on an industrial scale.
2.2.5 Self-organization of Feeble Magnetic Materials by Magnetically
Induced Dipole Interactions
The magnetic field effects described above are mainly based on magnetic
forces, and magnetic forces can be expressed as interactions between
feeble magnetic substances and gradient fields. In contrast, interactions
among feeble magnetic substances under magnetic fields have been

--- Page 88 ---

68 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
neglected thus far. In ferromagnetic substances, interactions through their
magnetic dipoles can be observed clearly.'' '^^ On the other hand, in feeble
magnetic substances, magnetic dipoles are induced only under magnetic
fields, and their values are extremely small. Therefore, the energy of the
interactions becomes too small, and they are not usually observed.
However, through well-designed experiments using high fields of several
teslas, it was confirmed that such interactions can be observed visually
even in feeble magnetic substances.'^^ It is known that magnetic dipole
interactions lead dispersed particles to some ordered alignments. However,
only systems containing ferromagnetic substances have been considered so
far, and such applications have been restricted to a few materials. The
applications of this effect to systems of feeble magnetic substances would
make it possible to control structures of various materials, which would be
useful in material processing. In this section, the control of structures of
feeble magnetic substances by utilizing induced magnetic dipoles
interaction is introduced.'^^
First, alignments parallel to the fields were observed. The sample
particles used in this experiment were glass beads (~ 0.8 mm0), which are
diamagnetic and have volume magnetic susceptibility of-1.8 x 10"^
Manganese dichloride aqueous solution of 40 wt% was used as the
medium in consideration of the magneto-Archimedes effect. In this
experiment, the superconducting magnet was set horizontally. The glass
beads and MnCb aqueous solution were put in a glass cell, which was
inserted into the bore of the magnet. One of the cell edges was fixed at the
center of the field, and the glass beads initially gathered at that side. From
this configuration, magnetic fields were increased gradually. Then,
magnetic forces acted on the glass beads, and they moved to the other side
of the cell to avoid the higher field region. These processes were observed
from the bottom of the cell. One of the results of these experiments is
shown in Fig. 2-2-6. In this figure, the field was applied parallel to the
space, decreasing from right to left, and its intensity was around 2.5 T. As
seen in the figure, the glass beads aligned in chain-like formations parallel
to the applied field as they moved away from the center of the field. These
formations were derived from the attractive interactions among magnetic
dipoles induced in the glass beads.
Alignments perpendicular to fields were then observed. In this
experiment, the magnet was placed vertically and a Petri dish containing
the sample particles and surrounding medium was placed in its bore. The
particles used in this case were gold spheres 1.0 mm in diameter; 40 wt%
MnCl2 aqueous solution was used as the medium. The gold spheres were
positioned 149 mm above the field center, where the fields were only
slightly larger (-0.2%) at the wall side than at the middle of the bore. From
this configuration, field was applied and the two-dimensional alignments
of the spheres observed from above. To avoid the effect of friction between

--- Page 89 ---

Magneto-Archimedes Effects and Related Effects 69
Fig. 2-2-6 Chain-like alignments of glass beads in 40 wt% MnCbaq. The magnetic field was directed
from left to right, and its intensity was 2.5 T at the field center.
[Reproduced from T. Takayama et al.. Trans. Mater. Res. Soc. Jpn., 27, 43 (2002)]
Fig. 2-2-7 Triangle-lattice alignments of gold balls in 40 wt% MnChaq. The direction of magnetic

[Reproduced from N. Hirota, Trans. Mater. Res. Soc. Jpn., 28, 231(2003)]
gold spheres and Petri dish, the field intensity was adjusted and the
apparent weights of the gold spheres were set to zero utilizing vertical
magnetic forces, i. e., magneto-Archimedes levitation was applied. Fig. 2-
2-7 shows the magneto-Archimedes levitation state of gold spheres
observed from above. The gold spheres gathered to the middle of the bore,
influenced by slight radial magnetic forces. However, the gathering
spheres were not closely packed and formed triangle-lattice alignments
with some spacing. This formation of the lattices was caused by the
repulsive interactions between each magnetic dipole induced in the gold
spheres. Alignments perpendicular to fields can be controlled by repulsive
interactions among induced magnetic dipoles.
As confirmed here, it was found that the formation of alignment was
attained even in systems consisting of only feeble magnetic materials.
Because magnetism is a property that all materials have, the knowledge
obtained here means that control of structures or self-organization will be
possible in a wide range of materials. Therefore, these phenomena suggest
new applications of magnetic fields to various fields such as materials
processing.

--- Page 90 ---

70 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
As demonstrated by the experiments described above, various new
processes can be controlled by the presence of magnetic fields. Since the
effects are proportional to the square of the field strength B^, making use of
a superconducting magnet is essential to boost the magnetic field effect.
Consequently, magnetic fields have a great potential to be applied to
materials processing in accordance with the future development of higher
field magnets or specially designed magnets for material processing of
feeble magnetic materials.

2.3 Formation of Protein Crystals under Microgravily on Earth 71
2.3 Formation of Protein Crystals under Microgravity on Earth
2.3.1 Why High Quality Protein Crystals Are Necessary
Proteins are the elementary building units of living creatures, and the
molecular structure of proteins provides the basis for understanding the
functions of biological macromolecules. Determining the structure of
protein molecules is therefore crucial. The most powerful technique for
determining protein structure is X-ray crystallography. However, the
production of crystals of adequate size and high quality is often the
"bottleneck" for three-dimensional structure analysis of macromolecular
crystals. The crystallization of biological macromolecules is an empirical
science of rational trial and error guided by previous studies, and the
development of general methods to improve crystal quality is essential. For
example, crystallization experiments conducted in space have indicated
that about 30% of crystals grown in space yield better X-ray diffraction
data than the best crystals grown on Earth.'^ An obvious difference
between space- and Earth-based experiments is the reduction of buoyancy-
driven convection and sedimentation of crystals.
Until recently, there were few methods to grow protein crystals in
microgravity outside the space station. The cost of space experiments is
expensive and the opportunity to experiment is limited. However, in 1996,
a new method to control effective gravity using a vertical magnetic force
was proposed.^ "^^ In the following section of this review, we summarize a
method and apparatus to control effective gravity ranging from
microgravity to hypergravity. Using the low gravity environments obtained
by this method, it is possible to damp buoyancy-driven convection even in
electrically low- and nonconducting fluids such as water, organic solvents,
typical protein aqueous solutions, etc.^*
For growing protein crystals, low gravity environments obtained by
the above method are considered to be the best candidates for substituting
or simulating the microgravity conditions in space.^'^^ In this review, we
describe the first formation of protein crystals in microgravity produced by
a superconducting magnet.^^ Finally, we discuss the future and potential of
microgravity environments obtained by this method.
2.3.2 A Method to Control Effective Gravity
Recently, a new method to control effective gravity has been invented.^'^^
Most materials, such as water and a majority of proteins, are diamagnetic
and experience a weak repulsive force along the steepest gradient of the
magnetic field. Generally, a unit volume of a substance in a one-
dimensional magnetic field gradient experiences a force Fm :

--- Page 92 ---

72 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
Fm =pi2oXgH(dH/dy) (1)
where p is density, /io(= 4;rx 10'^ H m~') is the absolute magnetic
permeabiUty of vacuum, Xg the magnetic susceptibility per unit weight, H
the magnetic field strength and y the site coordinate.
Because Fm is proportional to the material density, its superposition
on gravity can partially reduce or enhance the effective gravity (vertical
acceleration). When an upward Fm is applied along the gravitational force
Fg, the total force acting on a fluid inside a container is
F=F,-F^=p{g-\jUoX,H(dH/dy)\} = ppg (2)
This equation indicates that the level of effective gravity p can be
modified by changing the value of H(dH/dy). Most diamagnetic materials
have nearly the same negative Xg^ ^^^ Xg is independent of temperature.
Therefore, even for mixtures such as protein aqueous solutions, Eq. (2)
holds, and the effective gravity can be controlled approximately. Fig. 2-3-1
shows a cross-section schematic of the vertical superconducting magnet
which was used in the present study and the spatial distribution of the

Until recently, a large magnetic field gradient of Ho^H(dH/dy) =
-1370 T^ m"^ could be provided for only several hours by a massive hybrid
magnet. However, since 2002, a convenient type of superconducting
magnet can generate such a large magnetic field gradient. Specifically, for
the formation of protein crystals, generating a microgravity environment
for a long time is crucial. A magnet such as the JASTEC LH15T40 of
Japan Superconductor Technology Inc.'^^ and the installation of a magnetic
booster to ordinary superconducting magnet ''^ can supply a microgravity
environment for more than several weeks.
Protein crystal formation experiments were conducted using the
former superconducting magnet that can generate environments of
microgravity (|iG) at position A, 1 G at position B and 1.8 G at position C,
simultaneously (see Fig. 2-3-1).^^ Magnetic field strength, H and H(dH/dy)
at each position, are shown in Fig. 2-3-1. The inner diameter of the bore
was 40 mm.
2.3.3 Experimental Procedure
Orthorhombic lysozyme crystals were obtained by the conventional batch
method. Hen egg-white lysozyme (HEWL, Seikagaku Kogyo,
recrystallized six times) was used without further purification. A
supersaturated solution was prepared by mixing an equal volume of the
lysozyme and NaCl solutions to give a solution with a final concentration
of 60 mg mr^ HEWL and 40 mg ml' NaCl. The pH was controlled at 4.60
by adding 1 N HCl. The solution was then filtered through a filter of pore
size 0.22 jim. Immediately after the supersaturated solution was prepared,
it was separately injected into small vessels and placed both inside and
outside the magnet. As shown in Fig. 2-3-1, three positions were used (A:
|LiG/ll T, B: 1 G/15 T, C: 1.8 G/11 T) inside the magnet. Simultaneously,
samples were also placed outside the magnet (1 G/OT) as controls. The
temperature of the samples was controlled at 40.0 ± 0.1 °C in copper tubes
by flowing temperature-controlled water. The size of the vessels was
(|)6.5 mm x 16.5 mm. Solution volume in each vessel was 100 |LI1 and the
solution height was about 3 mm. The duration of the crystallization was
two days. After crystallization, the best-looking crystals of similar size
were chosen from different positions for analysis. X-ray diffraction data
were measured on a Bruker SMART6000 diffractometer with CuKa
radiation from a MAC Science MX06 rotating anode generator. The data
sets were processed with the programs SaintPlus (Bruker) and Madnes
(Nonius).
There are several parameters to indicate the quality of protein
crystals, such as the I/a value, overall ^-factor, mosaicity, /Emerge, rocking
curve, maximum resolution, etc. Some parameters are much affected by
crystal size and X-ray diffraction measurement conditions. In this study,
the crystal quality was compared using the overall 5-factor because it is

--- Page 94 ---

74 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
essentially independent of crystal size. Generally, the overall ^-factor is
not very sensitive to the crystal quality because thermal vibration mostly
contributes to this factor. Therefore, the change in overall i5-factor
corresponding to the improvement of crystal quality appears relatively
small.
2.3.4 Experimental Results and Discussion
Figure 2-3-2 shows the results of three experiments graphically, using the
overall ^-factor. The numbers in circles and triangles represent the number
of experimental runs. (Crystals were grown from the same mother solution
in the same experimental run.) From Fig. 2-3-2, crystal quality using
different comparison criteria can be ranked.
A. Comparison between Microgravity-grown Crystals and 1.8 G-grown
Crystals
Recently, the effects of strong magnetic fields on protein crystal quality
have been studied.^ ^ '^^ Currently, four mechanisms are known (see Table
2 in ref.^^), and all of them may contribute to the improvement of crystal
quality. Low-gravity environment is obtained by applying a magnetic field
gradient, while other mechanisms such as magnetic orientation of crystals,
damping of natural convection by Lorentz force, etc. work in both uniform
and gradient magnetic fields. The magnetic field strength (11 T) at
microgravity position (A) is the same as that of 1.8 G (C) (see Fig. 2-3-1).
Therefore, the comparison of crystals formed in microgravity and
hypergravity makes it possible to examine purely the dependence of
effective gravity on crystal quality. As shown in Fig. 2-3-2, in the three sets
of experimental results, the overall 5-factor of the crystals obtained at |iG

was always smaller than that of the crystal obtained at 1.8 G. Since lower

convection.
In microgravity, natural convection is effectively damped, while it is
promoted at hypergravity. Previously, we showed experimentally and
theoretically that natural convection affects the crystal growth rate of
protein.^^'^'^^ Here the results clearly show that natural convection reduces
crystal quality. This agrees with the dependence of crystal quality of snake
muscle fluctose-l,6-bisphosphatase on the effective gravity from 0.7 G to
1.3 G.'^
B. 1 G-grown Crystals in the Presence and Absence of a Magnetic Field
(15 T)
At the center of the magnet (position B in Fig. 2-3-1), the field strength is
15 T and the gravity level is the same as outside, i.e., IG. Therefore,
comparing the crystal quality formed at position B with that outside the
magnet, we can study the effect of magnetic field strength on the crystal
quality. As shown in Fig. 2-3-2, the crystals obtained inside the magnet
(15 T) show better quality than those obtained outside. This result is in
agreement with the previous results on orthorhombic lysozyme crystals.^^^
On the other hand, for snake muscle fluctose-l,6-bisphosphatase,^^ the
improvement of crystal quality was not clear at the center of the magnet
(IG/IOT).
C. Comparison between Microgravity-grown Crystals (|LIG/11 T) and
Crystals Grown Outside the Magnet (1 G/0 T)
Compared with the crystals formed outside, the crystals obtained at the |LIG
position inside the magnet were affected by both the microgravity
environment and the magnetic field itself. Fig. 2-3-2 demonstrates that all
experimental data sets reproducibly show that the crystals obtained at the
|iG position exhibit better quality than those obtained at normal gravity
outside.
Thus, both microgravity and the magnetic field itself improve the
quality of the crystals effectively and reproducibly. Further verification of
the combined effects of microgravity and magnetic field itself will lead to
a more general means to grow high quality protein crystals.
Furthermore, it is notable that the magnet can provide an
environment ranging from microgravity to hypergravity. It is well known
that microgravity is not always effective for improving the crystal quality
of protein.'^ As described in section 2.3.4A, if crystals are grown in
microgravity (|LIG/11 T) and hypergravity (1.8 G/11 T) and their quality
compared, it is possible to determine whether or not microgravity is
effective for the improvement of crystal quality. For example, when we

--- Page 96 ---

76 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
applied this method to the formation of cubic porcine insulin and
tetragonal lysozyme crystals, dependence of crystal quality on effective
gravity was not observed. Thus, this kind of test will be useful in the
selection of promising proteins prior to experiments conducted in space.
Quality improvement of protein crystals is challenging work. Until
now, there were few general means available for improving crystal quality.
The current work on microgravity is one of many efforts being made to
achieve a more or less general means to grow high quality protein crystals.
At present, the cost of microgravity provided by a convenient type of
superconducting magnet is not very expensive. The procedure for using the
superconducting magnet is not difficult. We can use microgravity at any
time and obtain fresh protein crystals. For these reasons, we expect that the
microgravity produced by a superconducting magnet may act as a
powerful and widely applicable tool for growing high quality protein
crystals.
In conclusion, the use of both a microgravity environment and
magnetic field generated by a vibration-free, durable superconducting
magnet will provide an excellent environment for growing high quality
protein crystals. Further research is necessary to investigate the potential
applications of such microgravity on Earth.

2.4 Control of Thermal Convection Using Magnetic Field 77
2.4 Control of Thermal Convection Using Magnetic Field
Chemical, physical and biological phenomena are affected by Earth's
gravity. Gravity induces thermal convection and concentration-induced
convection. However, this simple fact is not well recognized in many
cases. By applying a magnetic force opposite to gravitational force,
apparent gravity can be reduced, whereas apparent gravity can be
strengthened by applying magnetic force in the same direction as gravity.
Therefore, it is interesting to learn how gravity-induced convection could
be controlled by applying a magnetic force.'^ In order to clarify the role of
vertical magnetic force on thermal convection, we have undertaken in situ
observations of the thermal convection of benzene solution in magnetic
fields. Organic photochemical reaction is used to heat the solution locally
and visualize the heated solution.
2.4.1 l,2.Dicyano-l,2-bis(2,4,5-trimethyl-3-thienyl)ethene(CMTE)^^
CMTE undergoes a photo-isomerization reaction upon laser irradiation and
its color changes from pale yellow to red due to the formation of photo-
isomers (PI).^^ The excess photon energy is used for local heating of the PI
solution. Therefore, the photoreaction of CMTE is convenient for local
heating of solution and visualizing the motion of the heated solution.
A quartz cell (10 mm x 10 mm x 40 mm) was placed in a room-
temperature bore tube of a superconducting magnet (JASTEC, LH15T40,
vertical field). The solution in the cell was irradiated by a UV light from
an XeCl excimer laser (Lambda Physik, COMPEX 102, 308 nm) through a
hole (6 mm) of a copper shade attached on the outer bottom surface of the
cell. It was placed at the upper (10 T, -1300 T' m'), middle (14 T, 0 T^
m'^) and lower (11 T, -I-IOOO T^ m~') positions in the bore tube and at zero
field (0 T, 0 T^ m"'). The movement of the red PI solution after laser
irradiation (94 mJ pulse', 3 Hz, 3 s) was observed using a CCD camera-
video system. The locations where the color of the PI solution was the
most intense in pictures were traced manually.
Figure 2-4-1 shows sequential photos demonstrating the movement
of the PI solution in magnetic fields. The photos taken at a 10-s delay after
laser irradiation indicate that the movement of the PI solution at 10 T
(-1300 T^ m~^) is retarded compared with those at other magnetic fields,
whereas those taken at a 20-s delay show that the PI solution at 11 T
(-1-1000 T^ m~') stops at the upper part of the solution. On the other hand,
the movement at 14 T (0 T^ m"') is similar to that at zero field, indicating
that it is not magnetic field but magnetic force that is responsible for the
movement of PI solution in magnetic fields.
Figure 2-4-2 shows the movements of the PI solution after laser
irradiation in magnetic fields. The movement of the PI solution is divided

is about 0.4 mm s"' regardless of the magnetic field.
The removal of the PI solution is explained by the pressure difference
between the bulk CMTE solution and the PI solution, which is given by
the following equation.

"^^ (1)
(Apsolution )gh + (A;if benzene )(1 ///o )hBdBld Z + (jf PI " j^CMTE ) C(l ///o )hBdB/d Z
where Apsoiution is the density change of the solution, A;fbenzene the magnetic

height of the PI solution, g gravity, //o the magnetic permeability of
vacuum, B the magnetic flux density, dB/dz the gradient of B to the vertical

respectively, and c is the concentration of the solute. The first term on the

solution, consisting of the volume change of benzene due to temperature
jump and that of the solute due to photo-isomerization. The second term is
the magnetic susceptibility change of benzene due to the temperature jump
and the third is the magnetic susceptibility change of the solute due to
photo-isomerization. The temperature of the PI solution changes with time
as it is cooled by the surrounding bulk solution; therefore the first and
second terms are time dependent.
At zero field, the thermal motion of the PI solution is induced by the
first term of Eq. (1). In magnetic fields, the second and third terms also
contribute to the convection. The direction of the magnetic force on
diamagnetic CMTE and PI is upward at the upper position, which acts in
the opposite direction to gravity, whereas it is downward at the lower
position, acting in the same direction as gravity.
From experimental data, the temperature jump of the PI solution due
to laser irradiation is roughly estimated to be 0.4°C. Then, AP values are
tentatively esfimated using the experimental data in the literature. AP
values are estimated assuming that the concentration of the solute is
negligibly small. At zero field, AP is 5.88/z Nm'-. At the upper and lower
positions, the AP values are 0.41/z and 10.09/z, respectively. At the upper
position, the PI solution stays at the bottom for longer time compared to
that at zero field, since AP is reduced by one order of magnitude because of
a magnetic force of -5.47/i. At the lower position, the PI solution moves
from the surface faster than at zero field, and AP becomes about double the
value at zero field. The observation that the PI solution at the lower
position moves downward cannot be explained solely by the magnetic
susceptibility change of benzene. It could be explained by taking into
account the third term in the right-hand side of Eq. (1). As it lifts the PI
solution is cooled by the bulk solution and the contribution of the first and
second terms to the upward movement fades out. Therefore, if ;ifpi < ;I^CMTE
< 0, the third term, which is downward at the lower position, may induce
the downward movement of the PI solution after it is cooled to the
surrounding temperature.
The initial lift speed of the PI solution in the bulk solution should be
explained similarly by using Eq. (1). However, the speed is almost the

--- Page 100 ---

80 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
same regardless of magnetic field. From the laser power dependence of
removal time and lift speed, it is found that the influence of magnetic
fields on the solution at the bottom surface is slightly different from that on
the solution in bulk, i.e., floating solution. With increasing laser power, the
removal time becomes short and lift speed becomes faster in all magnetic
field conditions. However, at higher power the removal time becomes
almost the same, whereas the lift speed is almost independent of the
magnetic forces at lower power. Therefore, the above-mentioned lift
speeds are those under low laser power condition. It seems that the
solution near the bottom surface is slightly different from that in bulk
solution, as the former might adhere slightly to the surface due to surface
tension. As a result, the response on the magnetic force in the surface
solution might be slightly different from that in bulk solution.
2.4.2 Photoreaction of Diphenylamine and Carbon Tetrabromide"*^
Next, thermal convection of benzene solution was examined using
photoreaction of diphenylamine (DPA) and carbon tetrabromide (CBr4). A
blue dye (4,4^4"-tri(anilino)triphenylmethane bromide, TATPM) is
formed on photo-irradiation of a solution of DPA and CBr4.^^ Thus, this
reaction was carried out using UV light from a high pressure Xe-lamp. Fig.
2-4-3 shows the movement of a TATPM solution after UV irradiation of
benzene solution of DPA and CBr4 in magnetic fields. At zero field, the
TATPM solution generated at the bottom of the cell moves from the
bottom at a 10 s delay after the starting photo-irradiation and moves
upward with a lift speed of 0.4 mm s~\ In a magnetic field of -1200 T^

The behavior of the TATPM solution in magnetic fields is opposite to

that of DPA and CBr4, the movement of TATPM in magnetic fields is
explainable using Eq. (1). It is interesting that thermal convection of
benzene is controlled not only by its own magnetic susceptibility but also
by that of the solutes.
Thus far, the shape of floating solutions in magnetic fields has not
been discussed. In the case of the DPA-CBr4 reaction, for example, the
shapes of the TATPM floating solution in magnetic fields are considerably
different from that at zero field. Even at 14 T (0 T^ m'^), where no
magnetic force is expected, the shape of the TATPM solution is less
diffused than that at 0 T (0 T^ m~'). These facts indicate that even a
homogeneous field might act on the motion of the solution. This
unexpected observation could be partly explained by introducing a new
magnetic force based on the magnetic susceptibility gradient. When its
magnetic susceptibility is inhomogeneous, a solution is expected to receive
a new magnetic force, F mag,^^
FLs={l/2no)(dx/dz)B' (2)
where dx/dz is the gradient of x in the z direction. The force is dependent
on the square of a homogeneous field B and not BdBldz. This means that a
homogenous field also induces a magnetic force. If the magnetic
susceptibility of the DPA-CBr4 solution is different from that of the
TATPM solution, F mag may act on the interface of the two solutions. As a
result, the diffusive motion of the TATPM solution could be affected by the
force, leading to the shape change of the solution in a magnetic field. By
taking Fmag into account, we could partly explain why the shape of the
floating solution in a homogenous field of 14 T (0 T^ m'^) is less diffused.
Analysis of the experimental data including Fmag is in progress.
In conclusion, it has been clearly demonstrated that thermal
convection can be controlled by using magnetic force.

2.5 Magnetic Separation of Transition Metal Ions
2.5.1 Magnetic Energy and Thermal Energy
In order for particles to be separated by a magnetic field, the magnetic
force acting on the particles must be more effective than the thermal
fluctuation induced by a solvent environment. The magnetic energy
(XnHVl = 1.7 X 10"^^ J) of a paramagnetic ion (x = 2,000 X 10"^ cm' mo^^
l/n = 6.0 X 10^^ mol"') in a strong magnetic field (H = 100 kOe) is much
less than its thermal energy (3kT/2 = 6.2 x 10"'^ J, k : Boltzmann constant)
in a room-temperature solution (T = 300 K). Therefore, it has been
believed that the paramagnetic ions are diffused by the thermal fluctuation
but not separated by the magnetic force in the solution. Since the magnetic
energy of a paramagnetic particle of larger size (> 1.0 jim) is larger than its
thermal energy, many studies have focused on separation of the
paramagnetic particles.
Recently, we have succeeded in the separation of transition metal
ions in an inhomogeneous magnetic field.^^^ This section deals with the
mechanism of the magnetic separation of transition metal ions.
2.5.2 Separation of Metal Ions in a Magnetic Field
The magnetic field is horizontal and inhomogeneous. The field intensity is
80 kOe at the field center, and the field intensity x gradient is 410 kOe^
cm"' at ± 65 mm from the field center. The metal ion solutions were
spotted onto the silica gel support at 100 mm from the field center and
exposed to the magnetic field.
The separation of the Cu'^ (1,500 x 10^ cm' mol') and Ag^ (-24 x
10"^ cm' mol') ions in the magnetic field is shown in Fig. 2-5-1.'^ When
the magnetic field is applied, the Cu'^^ ions are attracted by -48 mm toward
the field center, but the Ag^ ions stay at the spot position. Fig. 2-5-2 shows
the distributions of the Cu^^ and Ag^ ions measured in the field direction
from the spot position. If the whole area is divided by a line at 10 mm, the
purity of the Cu^^ ions is -90 mol% in the region of > 10 mm, and the
purity of the Ag"^ ions -90 mol% in the region of < 10 mm.
The separation was also examined for the Co^^ (9,500 x 10"^ cm'
mol-') and Fe'^ (14,600 x 10^ cm' mol') ions and for the Cr'^ (6,200 x
10"^ cm' mol') and AP (-2 x 10^ cm' mol') ions in the magnetic field.^^

The Co^^ ions move by -58 mm toward the field center, but the Fe"^^ ions

of the Cr^^ ions is -77 mol% in the region of > 51 mm, and the purity of
the AP^ ions -77 mol% in the region of < 51 mm.
The magnetic separation of the metal ions is understood by the
attractive power of the magnetic field and by the adsorption activity of the
silica gel particles.
The Cu^^ (1,500 x 10"^ cm^ mol'), Co'^ (9,500 x 10^ cm^ mol"*)
and Cr^"" (6,200 x 10"^ cm'' mol"^) ions move toward the field center
because these paramagnetic ions are attracted by the magnetic force in the
direction of the maximum field.
The Ag^ (-24 x 10"^ cm'' mol"') ions do not leave the spot position
for the following reasons. First, the Ag^ ions have a small diamagnetic
susceptibility and are not affected by a weak magnetic force. Second, the
Ag"" ions are adsorbed strongly on the silica gel support and do not move in
any direction.
The Fe^^ (14,600 x 10^ cm^ mol') ions move less than the Co'^ ions,
though the Fe^^ ions have a larger paramagnetic susceptibility than the
Co^^ ions. This is because the Fe^^ ions are adsorbed more strongly on the
silica gel support than the Co"^ ions (Section 2.5.3).
The AP"^ (-2 X 10"^ cm^ mol') ions follow the Cr^"^ ions easily. Since
the AF"^ ions are not adsorbed strongly on the silica gel support, the Cr^""
ions, AF^ ions and water molecules form a group which is paramagnetic as
a whole and it is attracted toward the field center (Section 2.5.4).
2.5.3 Adsorption on Silica Gel Particles
The separation of the Co^"^ and Fe''^ ions is explained quantitatively by the
relationship between the magnetically moving distance and thermally
diffusing distance.^^ The different mechanisms of motions involve the fact
that the magnetic movement takes place as the motion of a group
composed of metal ions and water molecules (Section 2.5.4) but the
thermal diffusion occurs as the motion of a single metal ion.
For a group composed of metal ions and water molecules, the
distance z(t) of movement at time t in a magnetic field H(z) measures the
frictional coefficient/G (of metal-water group) and magnetic force F(z), the
latter of which is a function of the molar susceptibility x ^nd mole number
n of the metal ions (Section 2.5.4).

2.5.4 Movement of Paramagnetic Ions in a Magnetic Field: Theory
The movement of metal ions in a magnetic field is understood as the drift
motion of a group composed of metal ions and water molecules.^^ The
mathematical expressions are derived for the magnetic movement of the
metal ions.
Suppose that metal ions with molar susceptibility x ^^^ mole number
n are placed at position z in a magnetic field. The magnetic force F{z)
acting on the metal ions is expressed by

--- Page 106 ---

86 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
Fiz) = xnH(z)dH(z)/dz (3)
If the metal ions are paramagnetic (x > 0), the magnetic force works in the
direction where the field intensity increases, and the metal ions are
attracted toward the maximum field.
The movement of the n metal ions is described as the drift motion of
a group composed of metal ions and water molecules. The drift velocity
v(z) is related to the magnetic force F(z) by
v(z) = F(z)/f (4)
where / is the frictional coefficient which the metal-water group
experiences against a bulk water environment or silica gel support.
Now, it must be recalled that the position z of the n metal ions is an
implicit function of time /. The metal ions start from the position z = zo at
time t = ro and go across the position z = zi at time t = t\. The distance z\ -
Zo on which the metal ions move in the time interval t\ - to is given by
Z\-Zo = \''v(z)dt (5)
which is rewritten from Eqs. (3) and (4) as
zi - Zo =(xn / f)\\H(z)dH(z)/dz]dt (6)
Then the distance z\ - zo is calculated by numerical integration of Eq. (6).
The parameter//;fn is the only one involved in Eq. (6) and determined by
comparison with the observation.
The group of metal ions and water molecules is assumed to be a
sphere of hydrodynamic radius R in which the metal ions are contained
with molar concentration c. The frictional coefficient/is expressed by the
Stokes relation of
f = 6KriR (7)
where rj is the viscosity of the medium. The mole number n is related to
the molar concentration c by
n = {4n/3)R'c (8)
The parameter//;fn in Eq. (6) is written from Eqs. (7) and (8) in the form of
f/Xn = 9ri/2xR'c (9)
The right side of Eq. (9) involves explicitly the molar susceptibility x and
molar concentration c of the metal ions and enables one to estimate the
radius R of the metal-water group from the parameter//;|f«.
2.5.5 Movement of Paramagnetic Ions in a Magnetic Field:
Experiment
The magnetic movement of metal ions depends on the susceptibility of the

--- Page 107 ---

2.5 Magnetic Separation of Transition Metal Ions 87
metal ions.^^ The movement was observed for Mn^^ (x = 14,200 x 10"^ cm^
mol-^), Co'" ix = 9,500 x 10"' cm^ mol'), Cr'" (j = 6,200 x 10"' cm'
mol'), NP ix = 4,200 x lO"" cm' mol') and Cu'" {x = 1,500 x 10"" cm'
mol') ions. The solution containing the metal ions was spotted onto the
silica gel support and exposed to the magnetic field. The metal ions move
toward the field center, and the larger movement (Mn'" > Co'" > Cr'"^ >
Ni'" > Cu'") corresponds to the larger susceptibility (Mn'" > Co'" > Cr'" >
Ni'" > Cu'") for the metal ions.
The parameter//;fn was calculated numerically by Eq. (6) and plotted
logarithmically as a function of the molar susceptibility x of the metal ions.
(The parameter//;fn is the ratio of the frictional coefficient/to the product
of the molar susceptibility x and mole number n for the metal ions.) The
observed points were fitted with a line of slope of -1.11 ±0.11. Clearly,
the parameter fixn is inversely proportional to the molar susceptibility x-
This means that the driving force of the movement is the magnetic force
acting on the metal ions, as given in Eq. (3).
The magnetic movement of metal ions is dependent on the
concentration of the metal ions.'^ The movement was examined for various
concentrations of Cu'" ions. The Cu"" ion solution was spotted onto the
silica gel support and exposed to the magnetic field. When the
concentration of the Cu'" ions is higher, they move farther toward the
maximum field.
The parameter//;fn is plotted logarithmically in Fig. 2-5-3 against the
concentration c of the Cu'" ions. After the solution was spotted, the Cu'"
ions diffused, and the initial concentration decreased. Thus, the
concentration is estimated in Fig. 2-5-3 at 0 (initial concentration), 1 and
2 h from the spot fime of the solution. (The initial concentration at 0 h
decreases immediately and does not give an estimate of the concentration
during the movement of the Cu'" ions.) The observed points are fitted
linearly with slopes of-0.83 ± 0.04, -1.02 ± 0.05 and -1.01 ± 0.03 for 0, 1
and 2 h, respectively. It is clear from the slopes for 1 and 2 h that the
parameter//;fn is inversely proportional to the concentration c.
The movement of metal ions is discussed from the possible viewpoint
of the drift motion of the single metal ion. If a single Cu'" ion ix = 1,500 x
10"^ cm' mol', \ln = 6.022 x 10'' mor\ IR = 0.40 nm) is assumed to
move in a water environment (rj = 1.0 mPa s), the frictional coeflicient/is
calculated to be 3.8 x 10"'^ kg s"' from the Stokes relation in Eq. (7). The
parameter//;^n is predicted to be 1.5 x 10'' m"' kg s'', which is
independent of the concentration c of the Cu'" ions. This is in conflict with
the observation in Fig. 2-5-3, denying the movement of the single metal
ion.
The movement of metal ions is discussed on the model of the drift
modon of a group composed of metal ions and water molecules. If the
sphere of a metal-water group moves in a water environment, the mole

2.5.6 Magnetic Susceptibility and Adsorption Activity
Metal ions with paramagnetic susceptibility move by magnetic force, not
as a single particle, but as a large group composed of the metal ions and
water molecules. The metal ions are separated when they are adsorbed on
the silica gel particles. Magnetic separation makes effective use of the
magnetic susceptibility and adsorption activity.

2.6 Magnetic Force Used for Micropatteming, Separation and Processing 89
2.6 Magnetic Force Used for Micropatteming, Separation and
Processing
In this section, we demonstrate our recent work on the use of magnetic
force for the manipulation of particles. The section starts with a description
of the underlying mechanism of particle transport under a field gradient in
association with the Brownian motion and gravity, followed by examples
of application that include micropatteming of particles, separation of
polymers and polymerization of a large polymer sphere in a magnetic field.
2.6.1 Mechanism
Let us consider particles each having a volume V (m') and a diamagnetic
susceptibility Xv'> suspended in a liquid medium of a magnetic
suscepdbility x^- Flux of the particles takes place if a magnetic field
gradient VB^ is applied. In addition, if there is a gradient of the particle
concentration, a flux due to the Brownian motion takes place. The total
flux / is then written as the sum of these fluxes. ^^^
/ = c£(-yV^>VG + (2//or'A;fVVfi2) (1)
where c (mol m~^) is the concentration of the particle, e (vcc s"^ J"^) the

Einstein relation (D = ek^ r, with ^B being the Boltzmann constant), TVA the
Avogadro number, jUo the magnetic permeability of vacuum, B (T) the
magnetic flux density, lsx-X\>- X^ ^i^d G the chemical potential of the
particle defined by G = Go + RTln a^c with Go and a^ being a reference
chemical potential and the activity coefficient, respectively.
Substituting G (assuming a^ - 1 for simplicity, i.e., a suspension or a
solution is ideal) into Eq. (1), putting 7 = 0, then carrying out the
integration, we obtain the equilibrium distribution for the concentration
profile c. In the one-dimensional case, we obtain

where jco is a reference position. This is the Boltzmann factor determined
by the ratio of the magnetic energy to the thermal energy. It should be
noted that the mobility does not come into the equilibrium distribution.
Let us consider a simple case of diamagnetic particles trapped in a
harmonic well of magnetic energy: in Eq. (2) we put C(JC)/C(0) = exp(-ajc^)

sharper for particles of larger size and for the combination of the medium
and the particle that makes |A/| large.
The transient behavior from the initial distribution to the equilibrium
is described by Pick's second law, 3c/3t = -V • /. A quicker equilibrium is

homogeneous magnetic field because the magnetic flux density is

ferromagnetic material placed in a magnetic field.

2.6.2 Field Modulator
As discussed in the previous section, diamagnetic particles suspended in a
surrounding paramagnetic medium are trapped to the place where the field
strength is weak. Therefore, micropatteming of particles becomes possible
if a microscopically modulated field is available. We reported^' that a
homogeneous field is modulated in the vicinity of the surface of a field
modulator inserted into the field. Modulators composed of alternating
feeble magnetic and ferromagnetic layers, each having a thickness of 50 or
300 jLim, were used. Because the magnetic flux is selectively directed
toward the ferromagnetic layers, the field profile is periodically modulated
near the modulator surface. The persistent length of the modulation is
expected to follow the order of the pitch of the layer structure of the
modulator: the shorter the pitch, the shorter the persistence over the
modulator surface. A schematic diagram of a field modulator is shown in
Fig. 2-6-1.

--- Page 111 ---

2.6 Magnetic Force Used for Micropatteming, Separation and Processing 91
/ Sample cCD
Fig. 2-6-1 A field modulator and experimental set-up with a magnetic field. EM, P and CCD indicate
electromagnet, pole piece and CCD camera, respectively.
The location of the minimum of the field produced by a modulator
depends on which direction the magnetic field is applied from. There are
two cases: the field direcdon is (i) parallel or (ii) perpendicular to the
surface of the alternating layers. The field strength is lower above the
feeble magnetic layers in case (i), while it is lower above the ferromagnetic
layers in case (ii). This indicates that by switching the field direction by
90°, we can obtain the negative and positive patterns. The location of the
trapped particles depends on the sign of Ax = ;fp - 7m. If the particles are
relatively diamagnetic with respect to the medium (Ax < 0), they are
trapped at the place of the field minima. It is not necessary for the particles
to be diamagnetic and the medium to be paramagnetic. The magnetic
nature of the particle relative to that of the surrounding medium determines
the location of trapping.
2.6.3 Micro Moses Effect
The microscopically modulated field causes not only the particle
micropatteming but also the micro undulafion of a fluid surface.^^ This
undulation is referred to as the "micro Moses effect" in accordance with
the term "Moses effect" given to the phenomenon of surface splitting
under a magnetic field gradient on the macroscopic scale.'* ^^ The term
"enhanced micro Moses effect" can also be used corresponding to
enhanced Moses effect.^^
Figure 2-6-2 shows an example of the micro Moses effect. A field
modulator composed of alternating aluminum and iron sheets 300-|im
thick was used. A mixture of water, ethanol and MnCh • 4H2O of
composition of 2:6:1 by weight was poured onto a Kapton film (25 |j.m)
that was in close contact with the surface of the field modulator, then
placed in the center of an electromagnet (see Fig. 2-6-1). In the absence of
the field, the surface of the liquid was flat, as seen in Fig. 2-6-2(a). Upon
application of a field of 1 T, the surface was undulated (Fig. 2-6-2(b)) in

--- Page 112 ---

92 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
Fig. 2-6-2 Micro Moses effect. A flat liquid surface (a) is undulated upon exposure to the modulated
magnetic field (b). Pitch of undulation is ca. 600 Jim.
accordance with the field modulation. The modulation proceeded without
delay, following the change in field strength. The liquid used here is
paramagnetic due to the addition of MnCb, hence the hills and valleys
correspond to the aluminum and iron layers, respectively.
2.6.4 Micropatterning
Trapping of polystyrene spheres (20 )im diameter) suspended in a liquid
was carried out using the same field modulator used above.*^^ A droplet of a
suspension was poured onto a Kapton film that was in close contact with
the field modulator. In this experiment, the liquid layer was thick so that no
micro Moses effect occurred. Before application of the magnetic field, the
spheres were dispersed uniformly. Upon application of the field, the
spheres started to accumulate above the iron layers to form broad lines. It
took about 15 min to bring the spheres to a complete accumulation.
In order to enhance the trapping rate and the sharpness of the line,
MnCl2 was added to the suspension. This made the difference A;if = ;fp - Xm
large, resulting in enhancement of the trapping force. The polystyrene
spheres were trapped within a few minutes to form a self-assembled line
pattern, which remained upon evaporation of the liquid medium in the
presence of the magnetic field.''
We also used a modulator made of 50-|Lim-thick aluminum and iron
sheets. At this thickness, the width of the field minimum is 50 |Lim at most.
If the trapping power is increased by increasing A;^, the resulting half width
of the particle profile may be much narrower than 50 |im. In fact, only one
20-|im polystyrene sphere was allowed to be trapped in this potential
valley. The trapping was possible for particles of smaller size. The
patterning of Si02 particles of 1 |im diameter was possible using a 50-|im
modulator.

2.6 Magnetic Force Used for Micropatteming. Separation and Processing 93
2.6.5 Separation
Equation (3) indicates the possibility of a separation of particles based on
the difference in ts.plt^x- Magnetic separation of plastic pellets was
demonstrated.^^ Pellets of poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA, p = 1.18 g
cm~^) and poly(ethylene terephthalate) (PET, p = 1.41 g cm'^) were put in
an aqueous solution of manganese chloride (p = 1.10 g cm'\ ;f =1.7 x 10^)
contained in a glass tube. They sank to the bottom of the glass tube. Then
the glass tube was placed between the pole pieces of an electromagnet that
could generate a horizontal field of up to 2 T so that the pellets were
located above the center of the pole pieces. With increase in field strength,
the pellets levitated in different locations, reaching a maximum separation
of 17 mm at a BABldiZ value of about -20 to -70 T^ m~^ Other polymers
including polystyrene and polypropylene were also separated.
2.6.6 Levitation Polymerization
To fabricate polymer spheres of micrometer size, suspension
polymerization is usually used. Small droplets of a monomer suspended in
a liquid by agitation, forming spherical shape due to the surface tension,
are polymerized to obtain solid spheres. This method, however, cannot be
used to fabricate large spheres of centimeter size. A large droplet cannot
maintain its spherical shape in a suspension because flotation to the
medium surface causes it to lose its sphericity. Flotation can be avoided if
the densities of the droplet and the suspending medium are precisely
adjusted, but it is very difficult to find an aqueous medium having a
density as low as that of organic monomers. Even if one was found to meet
this condition, the droplet would move around in the container because no
trapping force acts on it, leading to deformation of the spherical shape by
contact with the container wall, for example.
Magnetic trapping is a promising way to eliminate these difficulties.^^
Using magnetic trapping, we succeeded in fabricating a large polymer
sphere. This method is referred to as "levitation polymerization." A benzyl
methacrylate droplet of ca. 1 cm diameter containing an initiator of
polymerization was stably trapped in an aqueous solution of manganese
chloride using a magnetic field gradient generated by a cryogen-free
superconducting magnet. It polymerized slowly to reach a solid sphere as
shown in Fig. 2-6-3. During polymerization, the density of the sphere
changed, hence the levitation balance was lost. Therefore, it was necessary
to change the electric current supplied to the magnet so that the t^pltsx
value was maintained to keep the sphere at the same location in the
suspension. Without doing so, the sphere became out of trapping before
complete solidification and the spherical shape was not achieved.

--- Page 114 ---

94 2 Effects of Magnetic Force
Fig. 2-6-3 Poly(benzyl methacrylate) spheres fabricated by "levitation polymerization." The size is
about 1 cm in diameter. The arrow points at a small sphere fabricated by conventional
suspension polymerization.
2.6.7 Conclusion
In this section, some uses of magnetic force have been described. Magnetic
force can be as large as gravitational force. With particles of smaller size,
for example, micrometer size, the Brownian motion becomes effective.
Temporal change of the concentration distribution of the particles under
the Brownian motion and the magnetic field gradient is described by the
diffusion equation. The solution to the stationary state is given by the
Boltzmann distribution, which is a function of the ratio of magnetic energy
to thermal energy.
It has been demonstrated that the use of a field modulator is useful
for the micropatterning of particles. A modulator inserted into a
homogeneous magnetic field creates potential minima where particles are
trapped. The microscopic undulation of a liquid surface (micro Moses
effect) is another application of the field modulator.
Two applications of the use of the macroscopic minimum created by
the balance of magnetic force and the gravitational force have been
demonstrated. One is the separation of polymer pellets, which may be
useful for plastic recycling. The other is "levitation polymerization," by
which polymer spheres of ca. 1 cm in diameter are fabricated.

2.7 Quantitative Evaluation of Solid Fraction 95
2.7 Quantitative Evaluation of Solid Fraction during
Solidification or Melting Processes of Metals Using
Measurement of Magnetic Susceptibility
Recently, magnetic force in nonmagnetic materials has become detectable
under a high magnetic field just like in ferromagnetic materials because the
technology required has become available. In order to develop materials
processing to make use of the high magnetic field, magnetic susceptibility
of materials must be measured.
Hitherto, the magnetic susceptibility of various materials has been
measured at a fixed temperature. A few continuous measurements
accompanied by phase transformation have been reported. ^'^^ The
continuous measurement of magnetic susceptibility will provide better
understanding of solidifying and melting phenomena and become useful in
the field of nonmagnetic materials processing.
The magnetic susceptibility can be estimated using a new apparatus
for continuously measuring the change in magnetic force during a phase
transformation. Based on the additivity of the magnetic susceptibility, a
new method quantitatively evaluating transitional solid fractions during
solidifying and melting processes has been proposed. This method has
been applied to the solidifying and melting of bismuth and zinc and
enables quantitative evaluation of the transitional reaction rate in various
chemical reaction processes and phase transitions.
2.7.1 Magnetic Susceptibility Measurement
A. Method
Magnetic susceptibility was measured by the Gouy method.^'^^ The
magnetic force of a cylindrical sample in a magnetic field Bz is given as
Eq.(l)
F.=\jmSdz^^Xr^[Bl-Bl) (1)
where S is the sectional area of the sample (m'^), L the sample height (m),

(kg), ;fm the magnetic susceptibility (m^ kg'), ^L and ^o the magnetic fiux
density (T) of the sample at the top and bottom parts, respectively. Taking
the weight difference of the sample measured by the electronic balance in
the cases with and without the magnetic field gives the magnetic force,
Eq.(2).
Fz^=M/-M. (2)
Since this magnetic force still contains the gravity and magnetic
forces contributed by the crucible and wire, it is not a pure magnetic force
provided by the sample itself. Thus, the weights (M'E and ME) of the
crucible without the sample were measured in the cases with and without
the magnetic field and subtracted from Eq. (2).

2.8 Magnetic Control of Gas Flow, Combustion and Oxidation
Reaction - Magnetoaerodynamics
2.8.1 Magnetically Induced Gas Flows
Recently, magnetic effects on gas flow, combustion and chemical reactions
involving O2 gas have been quantitatively studied, and it has been clarified
that the primary component of these magnetic effects is the existence of
magnetic attractive forces acting on paramagnetic oxygen gas. The study
of the interaction between electrically nonconducting paramagnetic oxygen
gases and magnetic fields is a new interdisciplinary research area called
"magnetoaerodynamics."
A. Magnetic Attraction of O2 Gas
Magnetic field gradients produced by ordinary electromagnets exert a
significant force on advancing O2 gas stream in air.''^^ When a mixture of
O2 gas and water mist is introduced through a plastic tube into the diameter
gap of an electromagnet, in the presence of a magnetic field, the oxygen
gas mixture is attracted toward the magnetic field and remains between the
magnetic poles. The water mist is used to visualize the gas flow. On the
other hand, in the absence of a magnetic field, the O2 gas mixture flows
downward. Thus, oxygen gas is attracted toward an electromagnet, similar
to a magnetic fluid.
B. Behavior of Diamagnetic N2 Gas Injected into Air
Magnetic field gradients have also been found to have significant effect on
diamagnetic N2 gas flow injected into air. A mixture of nitrogen gas
(700 cm^ min"') and water mist (0.2 g min"') was injected into air from a
glass tube. In the absence of magnetic fields, N2 gas mixture flows
unobstructed, as shown in Fig. 2-8-1(b). But when the gas outlet is set at
upper position A in Fig. 2-8-2(a) and the N2 gas flows toward a weaker
