4.2.3 Structure and Functions of Chemically Cross-linked Hydrogels
Prepared under Steady Magnetic Fields
Hydrogels have been investigated not only from the viewpoint of purely
scientific interest but also technological aspects such as drug delivery and
actuator. Thus, it is very important to investigate the methodology for
control of structures and physical properties of gels. Although the magnetic
energy of a nonmagnetic molecule in magnetic fields is much less than the
thermal energy, macromolecules having high molecular weight and
magnetic anisotropy can be oriented by high magnetic fields. Therefore,
magnetic fields may provide a useful method for the structural control of
gels. Magnetic anisotropy of a monomer or a polymer chain is not enough
to respond to magnetic fields, but polymer chains produced in the
polymerization process may orient cooperatively. When the oriented
polymer chains are cross-linked, a structured gel may be obtained. Gel
properties depend on polymer chain distribution, cross-link density and
hydration. If we can control these factors by magnetic fields, a novel gel
would be generated.
NIPA and AAm gels were prepared from aqueous solutions of
700 mM A^-isopropylacrylamide (NIPA) or acrylamide (AAm) monomer
and A^,7V'-methylenebisacrylamide (BIS) of various concentrations (CBIS =
4.3, 8.6, 17.2, 25.8, and 34.4 mM) in a thin glass capillary (inner diameter:
0.4 mm) at 278 K. The radical polymerization was initiated by potassium
peroxodisulfate and accelerated by A^,N,A^^A^'-tetramethylethylenediamine.
Steady magnetic fields (< 30 T) were applied perpendicular (Hi) or parallel
(H//) to the capillary for 60 min (during polymerization) using a
superconducting magnet and a hybrid magnet. Then the capillary remained
at rest under zero field at 278 K for 24 h. Cylindrical gels prepared were
taken out of the capillaries, washed repeatedly by temperature swings in a
large amount of fresh distilled water, then dried in air at room temperature.
The volume of NIPA gels prepared under steady magnetic fields
increased (Fig. 4-2-7(A)), and their volume phase transitions were much
more significant, as shown in Fig. 4-2-7(B). Magnetic fields increased the
diameter of the gels in the swelling state, but decreased the diameter in the
shrinking state.'^^ The diameter of gels in the swelling state increased
linearly with increase in magnetic field below 10 T, and became about 40%
larger at 30 T and 298 K than that of a zero-field gel.'^^ The volume phase
transition temperature did not change by magnetic field. When NIPA gels
were prepared at high /'^ (= CBIS/CNIPA, where CNIPA = 700 mM), the
diameter in the swelling state decreased continuously, i.e., no volume
phase transition appeared. However, when they were prepared under
magnetic fields, the diameter changed discontinuously at 33.8°C; the
volume phase transition appeared. The discontinuity in the volume phase
transition of the NIPA gels became more significant with increasing

Fig. 4-2-7 (A) Optical microscopic photographs of NIPA gels prepared under 0 T and 30 T. (B)

magnetic field intensity. The plots of the diameters of swelling and
shrunken gels at the volume phase transition temperature vs. magnetic
field intensity give the swelling line and the shrunken line, between which
the volume phase transition occurs. The swelling lines indicate that the
diameter was not saturated even at 30 T. The swelling and shrunken lines
of the gels were prepared at various q^'^ close at each magnetic field
intensity, /fv, where no volume phase transition occurs. In other words,
magnetic fields higher than //v induced the volume phase transition of
NIPA gels. The same phenomenon was observed in AAm gels, which are
well known as a nonvolume phase transition gel. Since the diameter of gels
increased with decreasing BIS concentration in preparation, it is inferred
that the decrease in gel diameter due to magnetic fields should be ascribed
to the reduction of cross-linkage in gels prepared under magnetic fields.
The effective degree of cross-linkage (q^^) was estimated by comparing the
diameter of gels prepared at different degrees of cross-linkage and zero-

--- Page 182 ---

164 4 Magneto-thermodynamic Effects
field with that of gels prepared under magnetic fields. Magnetic fields
reduced the effective cross-linkage, e.g., from /'^ = 0.0123 to q^^ = 0.004
at 10 T; generally q^^ < 1/3 /'^ over 15 T. Considering no change in C/N
ratios of gels estimated by elemental analysis with magnetic field, dangling
chains would be increased by magnetic fields. Also, the average number of
residues between cross-linking points increased with increasing magnetic
field intensity, which was estimated by the equation given by Flory. The
above findings suggest that magnetic fields reduced the network density of
gels to soften the gels.
Volume phase transition is subject to diffusion of the polymer
network in medium.^^^ The diffusion depends on polymer concentration
and cross-link density. Therefore, the decrease in the network density
under magnetic fields seems to induce the volume phase transition of high
q^'^ gels. However, considering that no volume phase transition was
observed even when AAm gels were prepared at low ^P'^ the volume phase
transition induced must also be subject to factors other than the network
density. The volume changes of Hi gels were much larger than those of H//
gels, suggesting that magneto-orientation of polymer chains in gels made
the gels anisotropic. The structural anisotropy led to a difference in the
iodine diffusion rate in H± and H// gels, which were about 10% faster and
20% slower than that of a zero-field gel, respectively.
4.2.4 Conclusion
Steady magnetic fields of less than 0.1 T brought about significant
responses in membrane potential and resistance. Deformation in lipid
membranes led to the magnetofusion and magnetodivision of the vesicles
and large changes in the electrical properties of their black membranes
under steady magnetic fields of up to 30 T. Pulsed magnetic fields also
affected lipid membranes very effectively. Micelles were deformed by
steady magnetic fields. Also, surfactant/silicate molecular assemblies were
effectively regulated by steady magnetic fields via orientation of surfactant
molecules used as template, leading to specific pore structures and
adsorptivity of mesoporous silicas. In these systems, magnetically
anisotropic molecules can effectively control the magnetoresponses in
structures and functions of molecular assembly systems. Chemically cross-
linked gels were also modified by magnetic fields in preparation and
regulated by applying external magnetic fields. These examples
demonstrate the potentiality of various magnetic fields that can regulate
the function, structure and size of artificially organized molecular
assemblies. This will lead to extensive applications for sensors, electric
devices, material transport, structure-controlled materials preparation, drug
release and numerous other uses.

4.3 Magnetic Field-induced Martensitic Transformation in
Ferrous Alloys
4.3.1 Effect of Magnetic Field on Martensitic Transformation
Temperature
Martensitic transformation, which occurs in many Fe-, Cu- and Ti-based
alloys and ceramics, is a typical first-order structural phase transition
without atom diffusion and has been widely studied to determine its
characteristics from physical, metallographical and crystallographic
viewpoints/^ In addition, the martensitic transformation has also been
studied from a technological viewpoint, partly because fine martensite

--- Page 184 ---

166 4 Magneto-thermodynamic Effects
Structures formed in quenched ferrous alloys and steels are effective for
strengthening and are thus used as structural materials, and partly because
the shape memory effect and pseudoelasticity effect appear in relation to
the thermoelastic martensitic transformation, and these effects are useful
for practical purposes as functional materials.
According to studies by Patel and Cohen^^ and Otsuka et al.,^^ some
aspects of martensitic transformation such as transformation temperature,
crystallography and the amount and morphology of the product martensites
are strongly influenced by external fields, such as temperature, hydrostatic
pressure and uniaxial stress. To clarify the effects of these external fields
on martensitic transformation it is very important to understand the
essential matters of the transformation such as thermodynamics, kinetics
and the origin of the transformation. Magnetic field is one of such external
fields because some difference in magnetic moment between the parent
and martensite states exists.^^
How the transformation temperature is influenced by the magnetic
field is shown schematically. Fig. 4-3-1 shows the temperature dependence
of the Gibbs chemical free energy of the parent phase (G^) and the
martensite phase (G""). At high temperature, the Gibbs chemical free
energy of the parent phase is lower than that of the martensite phase so the
parent is stable. When the parent phase is cooled, martensitic
transformation does not occur at equilibrium temperature, To, but occurs at
Ms, which is below To. This is because the interfacial energy and strain
energy are needed to initiate the transformation, and this additional energy
is supplied by the excess cooling from To to M^. When the magnetic field is
applied to the system, the Gibbs chemical free energy of the martensite
decreases mainly due to the magnetostatic energy (change in Gibbs

chemical free energy of the parent is neglected here for simplicity).
Therefore, the equilibrium temperature under the magnetic field increases
as shown in the figure. Also, the Ms, under the magnetic field increases to
Ms' if it is assumed that the martensitic transformation under the magnetic
field occurs at the temperature where the change in Gibbs chemical free
energies under the magnetic field, (G""- G^), is the same as that under no
magnetic field at Ms. This assumption on the effect of the magnetic field is
analogous to that on the effect of uniaxial stress and hydrostatic pressure
made by Patel and Cohen.^^ The validity of the assumption has been
confirmed by many researchers, that is, the effect of uniaxial stress on Ms
calculated by the assumption is in good agreement with the experimental
one in many materials.
In the following, the results of more detailed studies concerning the
effect of the magnetic field on the transformation temperatures in many
ferrous alloys are discussed. The samples used are three Invar Fe-Ni
alloys, disordered and ordered Fe-Pt Invar alloys and non-Invar Fe-Ni-C
and Fe-Mn-C alloys.^'^^ Their structural changes are basically those from
fee to bcc. High-field magnetization measurements were performed at the
Research Center for Materials Science at Extreme Conditions, Osaka
University, the magnetic field used in the study being a pulsed one with
maximum strength of 31 MA m"^ Details of the ultrahigh magnetic field
instrument are reported elsewhere.^^ Fig. 4-3-2 shows typical
magnetization curves (M(t)~H(t)) for Invar Fe-31.7Ni and non-Invar Fe-
24.7Ni-1.8C and Fe-3.9Mn-5.0C (at%) alloys, where Ar represents the
temperature difference between a set temperature T and Ms (AT = T - Ms).
In the figure, an abrupt increase in magnetization is recognized for all the
alloys at a certain strength of the magnetic field as indicated by an arrow.
The magnetic field, H^ at the abrupt increase in magnetizafion is the
critical one for inducing the martensitic transformation at r. That is, the set
temperature is interpreted as the martensitic transformation start
temperature, Ms', under the strength of magnetic field of He. The relations
thus obtained between the critical magnetic field and the shift of Ms, AMs (=
Ms'-Ms), are shown in Fig. 4-3-3 with solid circles for several alloys. It is
known from the figure that AMs increases with increasing magnetic field
for all the alloys examined, and the rate of the increase against the
magnetic field depends on the alloy system.
We have proposed^^ the following equation to estimate the relation
between the critical magnetic field and the transformation temperature:

in Eq. (1) under high magnetic fields. Recently, however, the
magnetocrystalline anisotropy was found to play an important role in
magnetic field-induced martensitic transformation in a Co-Fe alloy'^^ and a
Ni-Mn-Ga alloy.''^ Furthermore, this anisotropy is important for the
rearrangement of martensite variants, which will be described later.
4.3.2 Magnetoelastic Martensitic Transformation
In alloys exhibiting a thermoelastic martensitic transformation, each
martensite crystal grows or shrinks with temperature cycling, that is, it
responds elastically to temperature to maintain the balance between
thermal and elastic energies. If a uniaxial stress is applied to such an alloy
at a temperature above Af (reverse transformation finish temperature) and
released, the forward and reverse transformations occur by a loading cycle.
Thus the martensite is induced only while the stress is loaded and reverts
to the parent phase when the stress is released. Considering this behavior,
it is expected that if a magnetic field is applied above Af to an alloy which
exhibits a thermoelastic martensitic transformation and removed,
martensite can be induced only while the magnetic field is applied and
reverts to the parent phase when the magnetic field is removed. We found
this type of martensitic transformation in an ausaged Fe-31.9Ni-9.8Co-
4.1Ti (at%) shape memory alloy and gave it the term magnetoelastic
martensitic transformation'^^ in analogy to the thermoelastic
transformation. The term "ausage" means that the alloy is aged at a
temperature where its austenitic phase is stable.
Figure 4-3-5 shows the spontaneous magnetization of the parent and
the martensite phases as a function of temperature obtained by
magnetization measurements under low magnetic field. The difference in
spontaneous magnetization between the two phases is about 0.3 //e/atom at
Ms, which is of the same order as that in a previous Fe-32.5 at%Ni alloy. Ms
and Af shown with arrows are determined to be about 127 and 159 K,
respectively and As is 60 K. A pulsed high magnetic field was applied to a
sample at a temperature above Af, 163 K (AT= (T-M,) = 36 K) and typical
M(t)-H(t) curves obtained are shown in Fig. 4-3-6(a) and (b). It is noted in
Fig. 4-3-6(a) that there is no hysteresis in magnetization when a pulsed
magnetic field of 22.22 MA m"' is applied and removed. This means that
22.22 MA m"' is lower than the critical field He for inducing the
martensitic transformation, and therefore no martensitic transformation
occurs under a magnetic field of 22.22 MA m ^ A higher magnetic field
was then applied, and the M(t)-H(t) curve obtained is shown in (b),
revealing a hysteresis of magnetization. That is, when a magnetic field is
applied, the rate of increase of magnetization against the magnetic field
changes at He = 23.08 MA m"\ as indicated by an arrow, and when the
magnetic field is removed, the increased magnetization returns to the

Figure 4-3-7 shows optical micrographs of thermally-induced martensites

results (the morphology of a magnetic field-induced martensite was the

temperature and the strength of magnetic field) are obtained for Fe-Ni^ and

plates. The reason for this lengthwise growth of martensite plates under
magnetic field is not clear, but shape magnetic anisotropy effect appears to
play an important role.
4.3.4 Giant Magnetic Field-induced Strain in Ferromagnetic Shape
Memory Alloys
Sections 4.3.1 to 4.3.3 above discuss the magnetic field-induced
martensitic transformation. In this and following sections, another effect of
magnetic field on martensite phase is described. In some ferromagnetic
shape memory alloys (FMSMAs), the arrangement of martensite variants
can be controlled by a magnetic field. As a result, a giant magnetic field-
induced strain (MFIS) of several percent arises in these alloys.
Before describing MFIS behavior, the shape memory effect related to
MFIS is explained. When the martensite phase of a shape memory alloy is
induced thermally, it is usually composed of several variants
(crystallographic domains). For simplicity, we consider the case in which
the specimen is composed of two variants, as schematically shown in Fig.
4-3-9. When a shape memory alloy is cooled below the transformation
temperature, all variants will be equivalently formed naturally and they are
arranged so as to minimize the total shape change associated with
transformation (Fig. 4-3-9(a) -^ (b)). The interfaces of variants are
twinning planes, which can be moved easily by the application of external
stress T. As a result, rearrangement of variants occurs and a large strain
appears (Fig. 4-3-9(b) -^ (c)). This strain recovers by heating to the parent
phase (Fig. 4-3-9 (c) -^ (a)). This is the conventional shape memory effect
of shape memory alloys.
In ferromagnetic shape memory alloys, magnetic domains in addition
to variants exist, and the magnetic moment m will be aligned along the
easy axis of each variant, as shown in Fig. 4-3-9 (b). The easy axis of one
variant is nearly parallel to the hard axis of another variant. When a
magnetic field is applied along the hard axis of one variant which
corresponds to the easy axis of another variant, the magnetic energy of the
former variant becomes higher than that of the latter variant. As a result, a
magnetic shear stress Tmag will act across the twinning plane. The twinning

is the magnetocrystalline anisotropy constant, and it is attained when the
magnetic moment becomes parallel to the hard axis for one variant.
Considering the above mechanism, a large magnetocrystalline
anisotropy constant and small treq are essential to realize a giant MFIS in
ferromagnetic shape memory alloys. Based on this criterion, several
ferromagnetic shape memory alloys have been studied. The first report of a
giant MFIS was made by Ullakko et al.^^^ in a Ni-Mn-Ga alloy. After that, a
giant MFIS was also found in Fe-Pd alloys'^' '^^ and in FesPt.'^^ In the
following, we describe the MFIS in Fe-31.2Pd (at%), Fe3Pt and Ni2MnGa,
and in the subsequent section, we show that the relation rmag>rreq is clearly
realized in Fe-31.2Pd.
All three alloys (Fe-31.2Pd, Fe3Pt, Ni2MnGa) exhibit thermoelastic
martensitic transformations. The Fe-31.2Pd alloy transforms from the Al-
type structure (a quenched phase) to a tetragonal structure (so-called f.c.t.);
the FeaPt with degree of order of about 0.8 transforms from the Lh-type
structure to the L6o-type structure; and NiiMnGa transforms from the L2i-
type structure to the 10 M martensite with a pseudotetragonal structure.
The martensitic transformation temperature is 230 K for Fe-31.2Pd, 85 K
for FesPt and 202 K for Ni2MnGa. Fig. 4-3-10 shows the lattice parameters
of these alloys as a function of temperature. The lattice parameters of the
martensite phase in Fe-31.2Pd and Fe^Pt change gradually as temperature

decreases, but the change in NiiMnGa is small. Common to the three

easy axis of the Fe-31.2Pd is the a axis and that of the FeaPt and the
NiiMnGa is the c axis. Considering that the martensite phase is tetragonal,
there are three corresponding variants in the martensite phase. The
interface of these variants is {101}M (M stands for the martensite phase)
type twinning plane. The [001]? direction (P stands for the parent phase)
corresponds to the c axis for one variant and to the a axis for the remaining
two variants. The Curie temperature is about 650 K for Fe-31.2Pd, about
450 K for FeaPt and 376 K for NisMnGa. Fe-31.2Pd and FesPt are also
known as Invar alloys.

strain recovery in Fe^Pt will be caused by the accumulation of elastic
energy, but the reason is not yet known. The reversible strain exhibits
significant temperature dependence and reaches about 1 % at 20 K.
4.3.5 Energy Evaluation for Twinning Plane Movement by Magnetic
Field
Since the twinning plane moves under a magnetic field as mentioned
above, a kind of shear stress, imag, should act across the twinning plane
under a magnetic field. In the following we evaluate Tmag and compare it
with the stress required for the twinning plane movement Treq for the case
oftheFe-31.2Pd alloy.
In evaluating Tmag, we must know the magnetic energy difference per
unit volume AUmag between the two states before and after the movement
of the twinning plane. The magnetic shear stress will be expressed as Tmag
= AUmag/s, where s is the amount of twinning shear. Assuming that the
magnetic energy is mainly composed of magnetocrystalline anisotropy
energy and Zeeman energy, the maximum of AUmag is the uniaxial
magnetocrystalline anisotropy constant | Ku I when a magnetic field is
applied along the [001]? direction. The value of [KJ is obtained from the
area surrounded by the two magnetization curves along the hard axis and
along the easy axis. In the case of the Fe-31.2Pd alloy at 77 K, the value of
\KU\ is obtained to be 180 kJ m"\ The amount of twinning shear is
expressed as ^ = {1 - (c/af }l{cla). By substituting the lattice parameters
shown in Fig. 4-3-10, s is obtained to be 0.124 for Fe-31.2Pd at 77 K.
Using the above values of | ATu I and s, the maximum of Tmag is calculated to
be about 1.5 MPa for Fe-31.2Pd at 77 K. On the other hand, the value of
Treq Is obtalucd from tensile tests using a single-crystalline specimen and
found to be between 0.6 and 1.2 MPa at 80 K.
Comparing Tmag and Treq mentioned above, it is obvious that the value

--- Page 197 ---

4.4 Control of the Solidification Process by High Static Magnetic Field 179
of Tmag is larger than ireq. In this way, the reason for twinning plane
movement by magnetic field is explained by energy evaluation. The
condition Tmag > treq Is also confirmed to be satisfied for stoichiometric
NizMnGa (at about 80 K, r^ag: 3.4 MPa, Treq: 1.2-2.2 MPa, kul: 420 kJ
m-^5 = 0.124).

4.4 Control of the Solidification Process by High Static Magnetic
Field: Application to YBaCuO Superconductors
Since the synthesis of the superconducting YBaCuO phase in 1987 by Wu
et al.,^^ many efforts have been made on processing it to obtain a material
suitable for high current applications. The superconducting properties of
the orthorhombic YBa2Cu307 are highly anisotropic, and in particular,
critical currents are much larger in the ab plane of the lamellar structure
(Cu02 planes) than along the perpendicular c axis. Grain boundaries are
weak electrical junctions, and local measurements confirm that
superconducting magnetization currents loops only flow inside individual
grains. For high current applications, such as large monodomains for
superconducting magnetic bearings or long conductors for current
limitation devices, it is necessary to process large grained samples with
controlled crystallographic orientation.

--- Page 198 ---

180 4 Magneto-thermodynamic Effects
4.4.1 YBaCuO Growth
The YBa2Cu30^ (123) phase incongruently melts at 1015°C in air to form
the mixture of a Ba- and Cu- rich Hquid and of the YzBaCuOs (211) solid
phase (so-called "green phase"). During cooling, the 123 phase is formed
by a peritectic reaction between the hquid and the 211 phase. In a typical
peritectic recombination, the 123 phase should nucleate at each 211/liquid
interface on cooling, thus leading to a small-sized multigrain material,
since many green phase 211 particles are dispersed in the liquid above
1015°C. However, using high temperature optical microscopy, Rodriguez
et al.^^ demonstrated the nucleation of the 123 phase directly in the liquid.
Cima et al.^^ and Izumi et al.^' proposed a model where yttrium is dissolved
from 211 phases and diffuses to the 123/liquid interface to feed the 123
growth. Following Nakamura et al.,^' the growth speed is then governed by
yttrium diffusion in the melt and also by growth kinetics at the interface.
The growth is a slow process and typical values of the growth speed have
been experimentally measured below 1 mm h"^ The resulting material is
an YBa2Cu307 matrix (superconducting phase) including finely dispersed
insulating Y2BaCu05 particles.
4.4.2 Magnetic Texturation Feasibility
A. Mechanisms
High static magnetic fields induce magnetic alignment of solidifying
crystals. Two mechanisms, magnetocrystalline anisotropy and shape
anisotropy, account for this effect where in each case, a magnetic torque
acts on 123 nuclei freely suspended in the melt. If a solidifying single
crystal possesses a magneto-crystalline anisotropy, then its magnetization
M is generally not parallel to the applied field B. The sample is then
submitted to a torque r = VM AB^ with V the crystal volume, and rotates in
order to align its major (algebraic value) susceptibility axis parallel to the
field. In the case of a shape anisotropy, the demagnetizing field and hence
the magnetization depends on the angle between the field and the particle
orientation. The sample tends to rotate in order to maximize its
magnetization (so that it minimizes its internal demagnetization field) and
hence aligns its longest direction parallel to the field, whatever the sign of
the susceptibility may be.
In both mechanisms, the critical parameter for the occurrence of
magnetic alignment is the difference in magnetic energy between the
aligned and misaligned crystal as compared to the thermal disordering
energy kT (where k is the Boltzmann constant and T the temperature). For a
single crystal with magnetic anisotropy Ax, the necessary relation is:
R~
AEanisotropy = ^X ' V > ^T ( 1)

relation becomes:

A second limiting factor is the orientation time within the solidification
time. The orientation kinetic has been mathematically derived and
experimentally observed for a suspension of fibers in a viscous fluid.^' ^^ It
is found that the tangent of the angle between the applied field and an
orientation axis of the particle follows a decreasing exponential law with a
time constant T: tan(^) = ian{eo)e~''^. The time constant T is given for both
magneto-crystalline and shape anisotropy mechanisms as 7 = A/(2A£'),
where A is a viscous coefficient defined as the ratio between the viscous
torque and the rotation speed. As both A and AE are proportional to the
volume of the particle, T is independent of its size. A rough estimation can
be given for a sphere (A = 8 j^rir\ with r the radius and n the fluid
viscosity), then T = ^"^ must be much lower than the solidification time.

B. Magnetic Alignment of YBaCuO
As the anisotropy of Cu02 planes of the lamellar structure could lead to a
magnetic orientation at high temperature, the melting of precursors and the
growth and magnetic orientation of bulk YBaCuO material was then tested
in a high field and high temperature facility. A mixture of Y2BaCu05,
BaCu02 and CuO was heated to 1050°C, maintained for two hours in a 5-T
magnetic field, then cooled at 20°C h"^ to recombine the YBa2Cu307 phase
through the peritetic reaction.
After processing. X-ray diffraction pole figures were measured on
surfaces cut parallel and perpendicular to the applied field Ha. On the
surface perpendicular to Ha, (007) peaks are located in a very small area
parallel to Ha, while they are very weak in the other direction. These
results indicate that the resulting microstructure of the magnetically
processed YBaCuO sample consists of highly orientated crystals with the c
axis parallel to the applied field.^^ This structure is also evidenced by SEM
micrographs showing a layered structure where 10 micron-thick platelets
following the ab planes are stacked together.
During the recombination, nuclei of the YBaCuO phase have been
magnetically aligned in the surrounding liquid. Orientation by a shape
mechanism is rejected since the resulting microstructure should have led to
platelets aligned parallel to the applied field. The actual mechanism is then
the existence of a remaining magnetic anisotropy at the peritectic
temperature, with a larger value along the c axis.
C. Nuclei Size and Orientation Time
The magnetic anisotropy of the YBaCuO phase is associated with the

--- Page 200 ---

182 4 Magneto-thermodynamic Effects
CUO2 planes of the structure. The room temperature value A;f(300 K) is
about lO"'* emu mole"\ and is expected to decrease slowly with the
temperature because of the strong correlations in the Cu-0 layers.^^ The
critical volume for the magnetic orientation of a crystallite (A£ = kT at the
solidification temperature) is much smaller than 1 jim^: at 1500 K,
assuming a temperature independent anisotropy and with a 5-T magnetic
field, the value of the ratio ^E /kT is then about 5000.^^ The time constant T
is about 1 s in 5 T, even for a viscosity 10,000 times larger than that of
water. Considering the slow speed of the solidification process, the
orientation time should not be considered as a limiting factor.
D. Improvement of Magnetic and Superconducting Properties of Bulk
YBaCuO
First, magnetization cycles from zero field cooled state were measured
from 0to6Tat4Kona2x2x2 mm^ cube cut from the center of the
magnetically aligned sample and from a sample processed without field.
The sample processed without magnetic field shows no differences
between vertical and horizontal sample orientation in the measuring field,
in agreement with a disordered polycrystalline structure. The measured
remnant magnetization is then about 0.5 T. In contrast, a large anisotropy is
observed for the field processed sample, with a larger cycle when the
measuring field is parallel to the field applied at high temperature,
confirming the c axis orientation. Moreover, even the magnetization cycle
width measured in the perpendicular direction for this sample is larger than
the one of the zero field processed sample: the remnant magnetization
reaches 1.6 T along the direction of the alignment field and is still about
1 T in the perpendicular direction.
These figures demonstrate that the magnetic alignment enhances the
superconducting critical current density (which is proportional to the
magnetization cycle width) by taking advantage of the alignment of the c
axis and hence of the ab plane, which can carry larger currents. This
alignment also provides a better connection between grains, since both
anisotropic values are larger than in the disordered sample processed
without field.
However, these grain boundaries are still weak, limiting junctions for
large-scale superconducting current, and the number of grains needs to be
reduced, possibly to a single one, to obtain very large magnetizations or
long length conductors. In order to improve growth and reduce the
nucleation rate, the process has been optimized with the help of in situ
magnetic measurement at high temperatures.
4.4.3 In situ Magnetic Susceptibility in High Fields
A. Experimental Setup
When a sample of susceptibility x is placed in a magnetic field B gradient

--- Page 201 ---

4.4 Control of the Solidification Process by High Static Magnetic Field 183
region, it is then submitted to a force:
2jUo
Hence, by measuring the magnetic force exerted on an YBaCuO sample
during its processing, it is then possible to measure in real time its
magnetic susceptibility at high temperature and quantitatively determine
the phase transitions encountered.
A specially designed tubular furnace has been inserted in the vertical
room temperature bore of an Oxford Instrument superconducting magnet.
The high temperature zone of the furnace exists in a working alumina tube
surrounded by either a SiC machined tube heater or a heating wire
embedded in high temperature cement. The heater is insulated from the
exterior by a thin alumina/silica wool insulator, and the overall system is
inserted in a water-cooled jacket to remove heat and protect the magnet
cryostat.
The sample in its crucible is held on top of an alumina thin tube that
is put on the plateau of an electronic balance placed far below the magnetic
field. The sample temperature and magnetic weight are continuously
recorded by computer during the process. The device is conveniently used
for 25 g pellets, but larger samples of up to 200 g can also be processed in
a similar device. Due to the sample weight, the field gradient and the
balance quality, the magnetic susceptibility can be measured with a
sensitivity of about 10"'^ emu g"\ i.e., 10"^ S.I.
B. Standard MTG Process
The standard Melt Texture Growth (MTG) occurs in the slow cooling from
the partial melting state above the peritectic temperature as described
below.
The starting material is a pressed pellet of a mixture of YBa2Cu307,
Y2O3, Ag20 and PtOi powders. Y2O3 is added to provide excess Y2BaCu05
particles, which maintain the sample shape and are known to increase
critical currents. Ag20, which transforms into metal silver, is known to
reduce cracks, and Pt02 also enhances critical currents by reducing the
coarsening of the Y2BaCu05 particles. The sample is rapidly heated to
1100°C to transform all YBa2Cu307 and Y2O3 into a liquid phase
containing Ba and Cu and Y2BaCu05 solid particles. It is rapidly cooled to
1020°C and slow cooling (TC h') is then applied down to 890°C to
promote the peritectic recombination of the 123 phase.
C. Magnetic Signature of Melting, Solidification and Oxygen Exchanges
The magnetic susceptibility recorded during this process is shown in Fig.
4-4-1. On heating, a small increase is seen at 900°C and this is due to the
sintering of the system, together with a first transformation of Y2O3 into

reactions with the crucible and parasitic nucleations) but still promote
growth by slow cooling, the heat treatment has been modified as follows:
rapid cooling was maintained down to a few degrees above the previously
determined solidification point and the slow cooling applied only from that

grains, indicating rapid nucleation and poor growth. Although the
mechanism remains unclear, we suggest that since the rapidly cooled
sample was far from oxygen equilibrium, oxygen uptake occurred too
rapidly at that time, triggering a very fast and multiple nucleation as the
equilibrium peritectic temperature increased with oxygen content.
E. Double Dwell Process
In order to obtain both equilibrium oxygen and very large growth in a
reduced time, a double step process has been defined: the heating remains
unchanged, rapid cooling is still applied but at a few degrees above the
experimental solidification point, and the sample is maintained at constant
temperature until oxygen equilibrium is reached. Slow cooling of a few
degrees is then applied and a second temperature dwell is maintained until
complete solidification. In this process, the completion of oxygen
equilibrium and then peritectic recombination are evidenced in real time
by the magnetic susceptibility, as seen in Fig. 4-4-3.

Fig. 4-4-3 Magnetic susceptibility during the "double dwell" process. On cooling, the sample

In this figure, both the standard MTG process and the double dwell
process are presented for comparison. From 1020°C to about 955°C, the
susceptibility slope remains constant since almost no oxygen uptake is
allowed by the fast cooling, while a larger increase was seen for the slow
cooling of the standard MTG process. At 955°C, the susceptibility rose at a
constant temperature until it become saturated at a constant value,
indicating that oxygen had reached its equilibrium. Compared to the
standard process, the same susceptibility values were attained at that
temperature. The susceptibility increased again at the slow cooling until
the second dwell was applied at 946°C. Then after a few hours,
solidification occurred and the slow cooling was applied again after the
susceptibility decrease had stopped.
The resulting microstructure of the sample indicated a 17-mm
diameter single grain, and magnetic flux trapping confirmed the circulation
of the magnetization currents in a single loop at the scale of the full
sample.
The magnetic field has been used with two advantages in this study:
all phase transformations could be magnetically monitored to optimize the
growth process, and the single orientation was controlled by the magnetic
alignment.

A. Precursor Effect
In the double dwell process, the temperature profile was determined
following the magnetic susceptibility behavior of the material. A large
supercooling was observed in this case, with onset of the solidification

Defining a successful solidification process then requires careful
analysis of the magnetic susceptibility for each new precursor batch in
order to adapt the temperature profile to the supercooling behavior.
B. Overheating Effect
The supercooling behavior for a given batch and a given process is also
very sensitive to the maximum temperature reached in the liquid state. A
classical Melt Textured Growth process was applied at two different
maximum temperatures in the liquid state. A 45°C increase in the
overheating temperature induced an IPC reduction of the solidification
temperature.
C. Solidification Nuclei above the Melting Point
The solidification temperature dependence on the starting material and on
the overheating temperature suggests that the liquid phase, prior to the

--- Page 206 ---

188 4 Magneto-thermodynamic Effects
solidification, retains some information about the history of the sample. In
several systems (YBaCuO, BiSrCaCuO and SmCo), it was found that
magnetic orientation was also very sensitive to the maximum temperature
reached in the liquid state: a higher overheating leading to a larger
supercooling and a worse orientation.'^'
It has been suggested'^' that nuclei of the solid phase could survive
above the melting point of the bulk phase and that those nuclei may control
the solidification and orientation. With a low overheating above the
melting point, the nuclei remain in the liquid, are freely oriented by the
magnetic field and trigger the solidification with a small supercooling.
However, if a large overheating is applied, the nuclei are dissolved and
hence a larger supercooling is necessary to nucleate from heterogeneous
phases, such as the crucible walls, that cannot be magnetically aligned.
4.4.5 Conclusion
Bulk YBa2Cu307 superconductors can be textured by solidification through
a peritectic reaction performed at high temperature in high magnetic field
(7 T). The superconducting properties of the material are improved due to
the alignment of the ab planes and to a better connection between grains.
The high temperature magnetization of the sample in the high
magnetic field can be monitored in real time, following all phase
transformations in the bulk sample. An optimized process can then be
defined after determining the solidification temperature, which is strongly
dependent on the process and the starting materials. In an optimized
double dwell process, the oxygen equilibrium in the first temperature dwell
above the solidification point is probed by the magnetic susceptibility. The
solidification itself is then monitored during a second step a few degrees
below. A large oriented grain with improved superconducting properties is
then obtained.
High temperature, high field magnetization measurement is a
powerful tool to study the general phenomenon of solidification. Relations
between overheating, supercooling and magnetic orientation can be
experimentally investigated in order to test for the existence of
soUdification nuclei above the melting point.

5
Magnetic Orientation
Magnetic orientation arises from the anisotropic magnetic
energy of substances. Because of this anisotropy, a substance
receives torque in a magnetic field, orienting to the direction
where its magnetic energy is minimum. A single molecule or
ion barely undergoes magnetic orientation since its anisotropic
magnetic energy is negligibly small compared with the thermal
energy at room temperature. Aggregates or assemblies having
ordered structures, however, do undergo magnetic orientation
since their anisotropic magnetic energy exceeds the thermal
energy at room temperature. Magnetic orientation is currently a
very useful technique for industrial applications.
In this chapter, magnetic orientation of organic and
inorganic crystals, organic polymers, organic composites,
polymer gels, carbon nanotubes and other materials is described.
5.1 Magnetic Orientation of Organic and Inorganic Crystals
5.1.1 Magnetic Orientation of Crystals
For molecules having lower symmetry than cubic symmetry, the magnetic
susceptibility is anisotropic (dependent on direction), and the magnetic
energy is anisotropic (different by direction). The magnetic energy
anisotropy {^xnH^I2 = 8.3 x 10"'^ J) for one molecule {\ln = 6.0 x
lO^^mol"') with magnetic susceptibility anisotropy (A;^ = 100 x 10"^ cm^
mor^) in a strong magnetic field (// = 100 kOe) is much less than the
thermal energy {kTll = 2.1 x 10''' J, k\ Boltzmann constant) in a room-
temperature solution (r= 300 K).
When crystals are formed with an ordered structure by arrangement
of anisotropic molecules, the magnetic energy anisotropy becomes as large
as the thermal energy, and the crystals are oriented in the magnetically
stable direction by the magnetic torque against the thermal disordering.
This section reviews the magnetic orientation of small crystals. The
important conclusions are: (1) The magnetic orientation results from the
magnetic susceptibility anisotropy; (2) When the crystals possess a larger

--- Page 209 ---

192 5 Magnetic Orientation
size than a certain value (>1 nm), the magnetic orientation occurs against
the thermal disordering; (3) The magnetic orientation follows the
Boltzmann distribution for variation of the magnetic field intensity at a
fixed temperature.
5.1.2 Crystals in Magnetic Fields
The magnetic orientation has been observed for diamagnetic crystals, such
as benzophenone,''^^ rran^-azobenzene,^^ biphenyl/^ naphthalene^^ and
potassium nitrate crystals.^^ An example is presented for benzophenone
crystals growing in solution.^^ A supersaturated solution of benzophenone
was exposed to horizontal magnetic fields. The crystals appeared at the
interface between the solution and atmosphere and developed in the
horizontal plane. This shows that the crystal growth occurred in a two-
dimensional space.
The benzophenone crystals in the 80.0 kOe magnetic field are shown
in Fig. 5-l-l(a). They have a column form with dimensions of -10 x 1 x

crystallographic c axis (growth direction) is perpendicular to the field. The
crystals in the 4.8 kOe magnetic field are shown in Fig. 5-1-1(b). They are
oriented partially with the c axis perpendicular to the field. The crystals in

5.1.4 Magnetic Energy
Suppose a crystal is composed of mole number n of molecules and
possesses an axis Z of magnetic symmetry. When the crystal is placed in a
magnetic field //, the magnetic energy is expressed by
E(e,H) = -{nH' /2)[x±-^{X^-Xi)cos'^] (D
where 0 is the angle between the axis Z and field H {0<6 <K) and X\\ and
X± are the molar susceptibilities parallel and perpendicular to the axis Z,
respectively. If the susceptibilities hold a relation of X\\ < X^^ the magnetic
energy £(6, H) has a minimum, and the crystal is stable in the direction
where the axis Z is perpendicular to the magnetic field H{6 = KI2).
For the benzophenone crystals, the crystallographic c axis
corresponds to the axis Z of magnetic symmetry and the magnetic
susceptibilities bear the relation of X\\ < X^- The magnetic energy is
minimized, and the crystals are stabilized in the direction where the c axis
is perpendicular to the magneUc field. Thus, the magnetic orientation is
explained qualitatively by the magnetic energy.

5.1.6 Magnetic Orientation against Gravity
If the magnetically stable direction is different from the gravitationally
stable direction, what will happen? Can crystals be oriented by the
magnetic field in the direction that the gravitational field does not favor, or
can the magnetic energy be overcome by the gravitational energy? An
example is given in such a case for biphenyl and naphthalene crystals
growing in solution.^^
Biphenyl and naphthalene crystals have a plate form with dimensions
of-10 X 10 X 0.4 (biphenyl) and -10 x 10 x 0.5 (naphthalene) mm. (The
form and dimensions of the crystals are the same between the 80.0 kOe
and zero magnetic fields.) The crystals in the 80.0 kOe horizontal magnetic
field stand at 90 ± 1° angle (vertical), as if they were not sensitive to
gravity, to be oriented in the direction where the normal to the be plane
(plate plane) makes 16 ± 1° (biphenyl) and 21 ± 1° (naphthalene) angles to
the field. The crystals in zero magnetic field lie in random directions.
Similar results are observed for the crystals in the 80.0 kOe vertical
magnetic field. The crystals are oriented in the direction in which the
normal to the be plane is at 16 ± 3° (biphenyl) and 21 ± 2° (naphthalene)
angles to the field.
The crystal structure and magnetic susceptibilities have been reported
for the biphenyl and naphthalene crystals. The crystal system is
monoclinic.

5,2 Magnetic Field Processing of Organic Polymers and
Composites
Alignment of crystallites, polymer chains, fibers and other substances is a
key issue in many areas of materials processing.'^ A number of methods
including photochemical and mechanical methods have been explored to
achieve alignment. Magnetic alignment has an advantage over other
methods with respect to the uniformity, flexibility and range of materials it
covers.
A simple example of the magnetic alignment is the alignment of
fibers and crystals suspended in a liquid. The alignment of these
substances is described by a simple equation of rotation. Liquid crystals
also align, but the alignment mechanism is complicated because the

--- Page 214 ---

5.2 Magnetic Field Processing of Organic Polymers and Composites 197
aligning unit, which is clearly defined in the case of crystal and fiber
systems, is not well defined. The situation is much more complicated for
the alignment of anisotropic structures forming during the phase transition
that are frequently observed in many materials including crystalline
polymers, polymer gels, supramolecules and others. Anisotropic structures
arising during polymerization in biological systems also undergo magnetic
alignment,^' ^^ but the alignment mechanism appears complicated.
In this section, a number of examples of magnetic alignment are
demonstrated,'^ starting from simple fiber and crystal alignments to much
more complicated ones including the alignment of crystalline polymers
during the crystallization process.
5.2.1 Underlying Principles of Magnetic Alignment of Particles
A particle with anisotropic diamagnetic susceptibility is characterized by
three principal components, x^ < >^2 < ;fi < 0, of the diamagnetic
susceptibility tensor. Upon application of an external static field, the axis
corresponding to the largest component, i.e., ;fi, aligns in the field
direction. The other two axes automatically lie on the plane perpendicular
to the applied field, but they do not exhibit a preferential direction on this
plane. For fibers, two of the components corresponding to the direction
perpendicular to the fiber axis are equal. There are two cases: (i) x^ = /2 <
X\ and (ii) X^ < X^ - X\' The anisotropic diamagnefic susceptibility is
defined as the difference between the components corresponding to the
fiber axis and the direction perpendicular to it, that is, x^ = Xw - X^- This
value is positive for (i) and negative for (ii). Fibers where, x^ > 0' for
example, carbon fibers, poly(ethylene terephthalate) fibers, etc., align with
their fiber axes parallel to the applied field (uniaxial alignment). On the
other hand, fibers in which x^ < 0, for example, polyethylene fibers,
cellulose fibers, etc., align with their fiber axes perpendicular to the
applied field,'*^ but the fiber axes are randomly distributed on the plane
perpendicular to the applied field (planer alignment).
Alignment dynamics is described by the balance of the magnetic
torque and the hydrodynamic torque acting on a particle, whereby the
inertia term is neglected. The dynamics is strongly affected by the shape of
the particle because the hydrodynamic torque resulting from the rotation of
the particle is described by a tensor related to the particle shape. The
complication arising from the particle shape is reduced to some extent in
the case in which one of the principal hydrodynamic axes coincides with
one of the magnetic susceptibility axes. This is the case for plastic fibers
because the fiber axis (one of the hydrodynamic axes) coincides with the
direction of ;fii. The alignment mechanism is the simplest in the case of the
sphere.
In many application areas, the uniaxial alignment of the smallest
principal axis x^ is of great interest. For example, there is a need for a

--- Page 215 ---

198 5 Magnetic Orientation
uniaxial alignment of cellulose fibers. As long as we use static fields, we
do not obtain uniaxial alignment of fibers of jt'a < 0 but only planer
alignment. The rotating magnetic field is a solution to this problem. It is
expected intuitively that the smallest diamagnetic axis tends to align
perpendicular to the plane of the rotation, resulting in uniaxial alignment
of the smallest diamagnetic axis.^^ This idea has been confirmed
theoretically and experimentally.^' Recently, we extended this idea and
demonstrated that three-dimensional alignment of all the susceptibility
axes ;f3, Z2, X\ is possible using an elliptic magnetic field.^'
The balance between the magnetic torque due to the applied field and
the hydrodynamic torque exerted by a viscous medium determines the
rotation motion of a particle.^ ^* The magnetic torque N is expressed as
where B is the magnetic field and V is the volume of the particle under
consideration. Here we assume a particle of prolate ellipsoid with n
coinciding with the long axis. The hydrodynamic torque M acting on the
particle, when it is rotating at an angular velocity fl, is written as
M = -riLQ (2)
where TJ is the viscosity of the medium surrounding the particle and L is a
tensor that is dependent on the particle shape. In the coordinate system
embedded in the particle, L is diagonal, with its principal components
being Li = L2 = L± and L3 = L where the parallel direction corresponds to

hence the magnetic energy is minimized when this angle is 90°. This
condition is always satisfied for the field rotating on the xy plane. The
angle (p is the phase delay of the rotation of the fiber axis with respect to
the rotating field. The value of cp becomes constant after several revolutions,

achieved.
If the suspension is composed of many fibers, it is necessary to
consider the interaction between fibers. The interaction may include one
due to the entanglement between fibers and the other due to the interaction
between the induced magnetic dipoles on the fibers. Experimentally, it is
found that fibers can undergo alignment in a concentrated suspension. In
the absence of all these interactions, the following expression is obtained
for the distribution of the angle ^ between the director and the applied
field. Starting with the initial random orientation distribution of fibers, we

5.2.2 Magnetic Alignment of Crystals
The discussion made in the previous section is valid for particles having a
volume V large enough to overcome the Brownian motion. A criterion for
the minimum size necessary to exceed the thermal energy is determined by
V = 2k^TnJ\Xa\B~ (6)
With a volume larger than this, the Boltzmann factor determining the ratio
of the alignments parallel and perpendicular to the field becomes larger
than e. Typically, organic crystals under 10 T should have a size larger than
several tens of nanometers to meet the above criterion. If their size is as
large as micrometer order, their degree of alignment is very high even
under moderate magnetic fields generated, for example, by permanent
magnets. The build-up rate of the alignment is estimated by f^ defined
previously. This rate does not depend on the particle size (both magnetic
and hydrodynamic torques are proportional to the particle volume), but it
strongly depends on the shape. Particles with elongated shapes such as
fibers need longer time to reach the alignment because the hydrodynamic
resistance acting on them from the suspending liquid is large compared to
that acting on spherical shapes.
We now show some examples of application. A diffusion-type
polarizer was fabricated using the magnetic alignment of urea crystallites
dispersed in poly(ethyl methacrylate) (PEMA).^^^ The urea crystallites
suspended in a toluene solution of poly(ethyl methacrylate) (PEMA) was

--- Page 218 ---

5.2 Magnetic Field Processing of Organic Polymers and Composites 201
Parallel Perpendicular
Fig. 5-2-3 Diffusion-type polarizer-prepared aligned urea crystallites dispersed in a polymer matrix.
placed in a magnetic field (higher than 0.5 T) and toluene allowed to
evaporate to obtain a PEMA / aligned urea crystal composite film. The
urea crystal exhibits two refractive indices, one for ordinary light (/Zo =
1.484) and the other for extraordinary light (/le = 1.602). The direction of ne
coincides with the crystal c axis. According to the crystal symmetry, the
principal axes of the diamagnetic susceptibility tensor and those of the
refractive index tensor are in a parallel relation. The c axis of the urea
crystal aligns parallel to the field direction, indicating that the c axis
corresponds to one of the principal magnetic susceptibility axes, that is, the
largest one. Since the PEMA has a refractive index {rim = 1.4778) close to
no, the ordinary component of the impinging light is allowed to pass
through, while the extraordinary component is selectively scattered at the
boundary of the polymer matrix and the embedded crystallite. Fig. 5-2-3
shows the image from the polarizing source viewed through the polarizer
prepared by this method.
Another example is the alignment of a crystalline polymer induced
by the epitaxial growth of the polymer chains on the surface of the
magnetically aligned crystallites of a nucleating agent.^'^ Crystallites align
in polymer melts. However, higher magnetic fields are necessary to attain a
quick alignment because the melt viscosity is much higher than the
viscosity of suspensions. Isotactic polypropylene (iPP) was melted with
crystallites of A^,A^'-dicyclohexyl-2,6-naphthalenedicarboxamide
(DCNDCA), a nucleating agent encouraging the epitaxial growth of the p-
phase iPP, under 8 T and subjected to crystallization upon cooling.
DCNDCA did not melt but remained solid during this process. The X-ray
diffraction pattern for the obtained film exhibited the alignment of the fi-
phase iPP. This technique could be used for other combinations of polymer
and nucleating agent.
5.2.3 Magnetic Alignment of Liquid Crystalline Polymers
It is well known that liquid crystalline polymers align easily under

--- Page 219 ---

202 5 Magnetic Orientation
magnetic fields. Unlike the alignment of fibers and crystallites, the
anisotropic structure unit existing in liquid crystalline polymers
responsible for the magnetic alignment is not clearly defined because the
director profile is continuous. However, it is evident that there are large
regions, as observed under microscope, necessary to overcome the thermal
agitation. We may regard the director as being equivalent to the anisotropic
axis n used previously. The temporal evolution of the distribution of the
director upon application of the field is described by Eq. (5). In the case of
a liquid crystalline system, the interaction between directors is very strong,
that is, it is elastic, and hence the validity of Eq. (5) is much more limited^^
than it is when applied to the suspension system.
Control of the alignment is necessary to extract maximum mechanical
performance of liquid crystalline polymers at end-use products. Magnetic
alignment has a number of advantages over other alignment methods
including mechanical shearing and stretching. Because magnetic fields
penetrate into materials, the alignment is attained throughout the bulk. In
addition, the direction and strength of the field are easily controlled in an
arbitrary manner so that the graded alignment,'^' the alignment
perpendicular or inclined to the film surface, etc. are easily realized.
Magnetically and mechanically oriented films of a liquid crystalline
polymer, Rodrun LC-3000, composed of 60 mol% p-hydroxybenzoic acid
and 40 mol% ethylene terephthalate, were prepared and their mechanical
properties studied.'''^ Samples of varying degrees of orientation were
prepared by heat treatment of the film in a magnetic field (6 T) at
temperatures between 231 and 25 TC for 15 to 120 min. The thickness of
the film samples was ca. 100 |im. Since the magnetic field penetrates the
sample, the alignment may be uniform throughout the film thickness.
Mechanically elongated film samples were also prepared. The thickness of
the film samples was ca. 30-50 jiim, which was thin enough to be free of
the skin-core structure. This indicates that the measured mechanical
properties are intrinsic values for a given degree of orientation. In the case
of thicker films, lower values for the properties may be obtained because
the core part is likely to be less oriented than the skin part. This is in
contrast to the magnetically aligned film. The orientation degree was
determined using the half width of the X-ray azimuthal peak. The elastic
modulus, tensile strength and elongation to break were measured. The
comparison of the elastic modulus demonstrated that the magnetic method
was as powerful as mechanical method. The other mechanical properties
exhibited differences between magnetic and mechanical methods. These
differences were attributed to the difference in microscopic morphology.
5.2.4 Magnetic Alignment of Crystalline Polymers
Unlike liquid crystalline polymers, crystalline polymers were considered to
be unable to align under magnetic fields because they lack stable

--- Page 220 ---

5.2 Magnetic Field Processing of Organic Polymers and Composites 203
anisotropic structures, such as liquid crystalline phases, capable of
responding to the applied field. However, we have found'^ that many
crystalline polymers including poly(ethylene-2,6-naphthalate), isotactic
polystyrene,'"^^ isotactic polypropylene, poly (ethylene terephthalate),
paraffin, poly(ethylene oxide), polyetherester, polycarbonate and cellulose
triacetate, align magnetically under the appropriate conditions.
The alignment occurs during the transition between solid and liquid
such as crystallization from melt as well as melting from crystal. In line
with the previous discussion about the magnetic alignment of fibers,
crystallites and liquid crystalline polymers, we should assume that some
anisotropic structures of appropriate size surrounded by a low viscosity
circumstance exists or forms during the transition. In the field of polymer
crystallization, anistropic structures forming during the induction period of
crystallization are an issue of intense discussions.'''^ These structures, which
we refer to as the mesophase, may be responsible for the magnetic
alignment. The mesophase may include crystal embryos, condis crystals,
nematic-like structures, etc. Current understanding of the magnetic
alignment of crystalline polymers is as follows. The mesophase existing or
forming during the transition between the melt and the crystal aligns upon
exposure to the magnetic field. This oriented mesophase acts as the nuclei
of subsequent crystallization, resulting in the formation of aligned
crystallites.
A general thermal scheme used to attain magnetic alignment during
melt crystallization is shown in Fig. 5-2-4. Here, the field is applied
throughout the entire process. A solid sample is heated from room
temperature to a maximum melting temperature Tmax above the melting
point Tm. Then the sample is kept melted for a period of fm, followed by
cooling to the temperature T^ for the crystallization. An in situ magnetic
birefringence measurement was carried out for poly(ethylene
terephthalate) (PET) under 6 T.'^' The parameters for the thermal treatment
were T^ = 26 rC, r^ax = 270°C, T, = 250°C and tm = 5 min. A polarizer and
an analyzer were set under the crossed polar condition, each forming an
angle of 45° or -45° with respect to the vertical magnetic field of 6 T.
Under this condition, the transmitting light intensity is proportional to
sin\KdAn/X), where d is the sample thickness. An is the birefringence and A
is the wavelength (632.8 nm) of the impinging light (He-Ne laser). The
quantity dAn is referred to as retardation. If the increase in An due to the
magnetic alignment of the polymer chain is large, the intensity could
oscillate.
The in situ birefringence measurement exhibited oscillation, starting
at a very early stage of crystallization (crystallization time of ca. 5 min),
clearly indicating that the alignment occurred. In order to investigate at
which stage of crystallization the alignment started, wide angle X-ray
measurements were carried out for the samples quenched at various times

--- Page 221 ---

204 5 Magnetic Orientation
4
Melt
B
Crystallization
/^ •/ Glass
Time
Fig. 5-2-4 A general thermal scheme for the magnetic alignment of crystalline polymers.
of crystallization. The X-ray results demonstrated that the crystal
formation detectable by X-ray diffraction started at around 20 min. This
was far later than the start of the alignment detected by the birefringence
method (ca. 5 min). This observation leads us to the conclusion that the
origin of the alignment is not the crystal (at least as detectable by X-rays)
but the mesophase.
5.2.5 Nonrotation T^pe Magnetic Alignment of Crystalline Polymers
So far the alignment mechanism is described in terms of the magnetic
torque acting on a magnetically anisotropic particle. However, this is not
the only way of describing the phenomenon. Another possibility, referred
to as preferential alignment,'^^ is based on the shift in the transition
temperature due to the applied magnetic field. The shift in this model is
similar to the shift in the melting point due to the pressure. The chemical
potential of the mesophase depends on the mutual direction of the
anisotropic axis of the mesophase with respect to the direction of the
applied field. Therefore, there are two intersections corresponding to the
melting points of the mesophase aligned parallel and perpendicular to the
applied field, respectively. This results in a preferential melting of the
mesophase of lower melting point upon heating and a preferential
crystallization of the mesophase of higher melting point upon cooling,
leading to alignment.
The details of the model are as follows. The model assumes that (i) a
stable mesophase exists between the melt and crystalline phases, (ii)
intersection of the crystal- and mesophase-chemical potential lines and the
intersection of the mesophase- and melt-chemical potential lines define the
melting point Tm (determined by conventional thermal analysis) and the
equilibrium melting point Tm, respectively, (iii) the transition enthalpy AH
from mesophase to melt is extremely small compared to that from crystal
to mesophase and (iv) the shift in T^ due to the presence of the magnetic
field as expressed by the Magneto-Clapeyron equation.'^^ On the basis of

from the figure that the magnetic field preferentially accelerates the growth
of the mesophase with a parallel orientation to the field (in the case of ;ifa >
0), while the growth rate of the mesophase with a perpendicular orientation
is suppressed. This tendency is especially enhanced in the vicinity of the
equilibrium melting point. In addition, the growth rate for the parallel
orientation is larger than that in the absence of the field, indicating that the
crystallization is apparently accelerated in the magnetic field. Similar
statements are also valid for the primary nucleation. Incidentally, magnetic
aUgnment experiments are usually carried out at ca. TR = 0.9 to 0.95.
5.2.6 Conclusion
Magnetic alignment of feeble magnetic materials has been described.

--- Page 223 ---

206 5 Magnetic Orientation
Particle alignment is governed by factors including field strength, viscosity
of the surrounding medium, and the size, shape and magnetic anisotropy of
the particle. Of course, high fields (10 T or higher) are better, especially
when trying to find new phenomena, but moderate fields such as those
obatained by permanent magnets are sufficient to align particles if other
parameters such as the size of the particle are in favor of the alignment.
For typical anisotropic diamagnetic particles (j^a of 10"^), the minimum size
required to overcome the thermal agitation under 10 T is of the order of
sub-micrometers. The viscosity of the surrounding medium determines the
alignment speed. The volume of the particle does not come into the
alignment speed because both magnetic and hydrodynamic torques are
proportional to the volume. In a medium with low viscosity such as water,
the particle aligns almost instantaneously. The shape of the particle affects
the alignment speed. The speed is reduced by the square of the aspect ratio
of the fiber in comparison to the sphere. The alignment of particles of
micrometer size with large x^ suspended in a low viscosity medium,
followed by solidification of the medium may be best fitted to industrial
processes. This will work with a moderate field.
Magnetic alignment of anisotropic phases forming during the phase
transition is complicated compared to the particle alignment because the
size, shape and magnetic anisotropy of the aligning unit can change during
the transition. In addition, another possible mechanism is proposed. A
preferential crystallization or melting could occur because the stability of
the phase depends on the orientation direction of the anisotropic phase
with respect to the field. In order for this phenomenon to happen, the
transition enthalpy must be very small. It appears that no experimental
evidence has been presented so far.
Time-dependent magnetic fields such as circular and elliptic
magnetic fields are unique and useful for achieving alignment which is
never possible with a static magnetic field. The circular field enables the
alignment of hard magnetization axis in the direction perpendicular to the
circulating plane. The elliptic magnetic field enables the three-dimensional
alignment of the susceptibility axes, making it possible to turn crystalline
powders into a pseudo-single crystal.

5.3 Magnetic Alignment of Polymer Gels
5.3.1 Polymer Gels
The magnetic alignment, magnetic field effects on the phase transition and
some applications are introduced for polymer gels. It is expected that
magnetic energy becomes large enough to induce some chemical and
physical effects if a species of high molecular weight is exposed to a
strong magnetic field. Polymers consist of long chains of organic
compounds and are diamagnetic with anisotropic susceptibility. This
magnetic property leads to magnetically aligned polymers, e.g., liquid
crystal, agarose gel and fibrinogen protein.' ^^ The best known natural
vegetable gel is agar, whose main component is agarose. The
polysaccharide agarose consists of P-D-galactose and 3,6-anhydoro-a-L-
galactose. The agarose gel is used for cell cultivation in the field of biology
and for the supporting medium of the electrophoresis in the field of
medicine. Agarose is classified into several types according to the sol-gel
phase transition temperature and elasticity. The aqueous solution shows a
thermo-reversible sol-gel phase transition with a thermal hysteresis. The
agarose gel melts to form a solution at elevated temperatures and gelation
occurs at room temperature. A gelation model in the cooling process has
been suggested^^: the shape of the chains is changed from a random coil at
elevated temperatures to an a-helix at moderate temperatures. Then, the
two helices intertwine to form the double helix. Many double helices are
assembled and aligned to form high concentration regions called domains.
Linkages between the domains are generated and the solution is progresses
to form the gel at the gelation temperature. The transition temperature is
about Tg = 310 K for gelation, and Tm = 350 K for melting for the typical
agarose solution. In addition, the agarose gel indicates the gel-gel volume
transition in acetone aqueous solution: the gel shrinks in a high
concentration solution.

--- Page 225 ---

208 5 Magnetic Orientation
Interesting phase transitions have been reported for other gels. For
example, aqueous solution of methyl cellulose forms the gel at elevated
temperatures in contrast to the agarose gel. Acrylamid gel exhibits a large
volume transition: the gel shrinks below 30-40°C and the volume ratio
reaches over 100. Other factors such as electric field and pH can lead to
phase transitions of the gel. Many functional gels are under development.
5.3.2 Magnetic Field Effect for Agarose Gel
The gel-sol phase transition temperatures were investigated for the agarose
gel formed in magnetic fields. Cryocooled superconducting magnets (Type
SM-5, Sumitomo Heavy Industries Ltd. and Type JMTD-10 TlOO NC-
mkll, JASTEC) and the hybrid magnet (NIMS, Tsukuba) were used to
apply the magnetic fields. The temperature was controlled to form the gel
by using a water jacket set in the bore of the magnet. Homogeneity of the
magnetic field was less than 3% in a space of ±25 mm at the center of the
magnet. The raw materials used were Agarose type II with a gel strength of
6000-8000 kg m-^ and type L with 4500 kg m' supplied by Wako
Chemicals. They are a special grade for electrophoresis. The molecular
weight was 55 x 10^ gram. The mixture of the agarose powder and
distilled water was prepared at various concentrations of up to 4.0 wt% and
stirred at room temperature. Five thermal cycles, heating and cooling, were
repeated for homogenization before measurement.
5.3.3 Magnetic Field Effects on Phase Transition Temperature
A hot solution of agarose II was poured into a Pyrex-glass tube with ID =
3 mm, OD = 5 mm and / = 50 mm. Each sample tube enclosed a stainless-
steel made micro-ball with a diameter of 2 mm to determine the melting
temperature by the ball-drop method. Then the inlet of the tube was sealed
by burner. The sample tubes containing the hot solution were set in a water
jacket in the bore of the vertical type magnet to control the temperature.
Ten samples were placed at the center of the magnet and the other ten 600
mm below the center of the magnet, the bottom of the water jacket. When
the applied magnetic field is 5 T at the center, the field is less than 0.01 T
at the bottom position with no magnetic field. The samples were cooled to
form the gel at a rate of -0.3 K min"' through the water jacket by
temperature-controlled water. After gelation the twenty samples were taken
out of the magnet at zero field at room temperature. Then the tubes were
turned over and set into a hot-water bath controlled at room temperature.
The melting temperature of the gel was measured by the ball-dropping
method. With increasing bath temperature at a rate of +0.03 K, the gel
changed to the sol at melting temperature. Then the micro-ball dropped,
taking about 0.5 s. The melting temperature was determined at the moment
the micro-ball dropped 20 mm from the original position.
Figure 5-3-1 shows the magnetic field dependence of the melting

evidence of the magnetic alignment of agarose molecules. The magnetic
alignment depends on the strength of the field exposed and the
concentration of the gel.
5.3.5 Applications
Changes in the melting temperature and the birefringence suggest
anisotropic change in the network structure of the gel. The agarose gel
shows a volume transition in the acetone-water system. Volume expansion
and shrinkage is observed at around 70 wt% acetone. The volume
transition was measured for 4 wt% agarose gel formed in the magnetic
field of 10 T. In the experiment, the gel was prepared in capillary tubes
with ID = 1.0 mm and / = 50 mm, and the tubes were situated parallel or
perpendicular to the magnetic field of 10 T. The length of the gel was
measured as a function of acetone concentration at zero field at room
temperature. The length decreased with increasing acetone concentration
for gels of both tubes, and remarkable shrink was observed at a
concentration of 70 wt%, as shown in Fig. 5-3-4.^^^ Both shrink ratios were
saturated at around 80 wt%. The length of the gels after the shrinkage
differed from each other: the lengths parallel and perpendicular to the
magnetic field were 92% and 94%, respectively. This anisotropic
shrinkage occurs in the structure of the anisotropic network rather than in
the anisotropic structure of the domain region of the gel. The viscoelastic
measurements using an ultrasonic wave indicated anisotropic increase in
the elasticy in the gel and the hard direction was perpendicular to the
magnetic field exposed. The same tendency of anisotropic volumetric
phase transition was discovered for the NIPA gels. Therefore, the direction

molecules but also the anisotropic network structure of the gel. Indeed, the

decreased under the influence of the magnetic field because the DNA was
aligned perpendicularly to the magnetic field.*^' ^'^^ A new separation
method of DNA is anticipated using these unique alignment properties of
gel and DNA. Use of magnetically aligned gel expands the applications of
gel.

5.4 Magnetic Alignment of Multi-wall Carbon Nanotubes
5.4.1 Orientation of Carbon Nanotubes
Carbon nanotubes are large (> 1 jim) and have an ordered (anisotropic)
structure. They satisfy the conditions for magnetic orientation (Section
5.1.1) and are indeed oriented in magnetic fields.^^^
A suspension of carbon nanotubes was placed in horizontal magnetic
fields, and the solvent was vaporized. The scanning electron microscope
(SEM) image of the nanotubes in the 80.0 kOe magnetic field is shown in
Fig. 5-4-1(a). The nanotubes are 1.5 ± 0.6 |im in length and are oriented
with the tube axis parallel to the field. The spherical regions seem to be
amorphous graphitic nanoparticles produced as by-products in carbon arc
discharge. An SEM image of the nanotubes in zero field is shown in Fig. 5-
4-1(b). The nanotubes are directed randomly.
A transmission electron microscope (TEM) image of nanotubes was
observed. The outermost and innermost tubules are 17.9 ± 4.4 and 2.8 ±
1.2 nm in diameter, respectively. The wall thickness is 23 ± 7 sheets, and
the intershell distance is 0.349 ± 0.005 nm. On the assumption that the
concentric tubules have a uniform length within the nanotube, the mole
number of carbon atoms contained in the nanotube is calculated to be
Az = (7.6±4.4)xl0-*'mol
The magnetic orientation of the carbon nanotubes is analyzed
qualitatively by the magnetic anisotropy energy (Section 5.1.4). The
nanotube is magnetically symmetric along the tube axis and possesses
molar susceptibilities parallel (jfii) and perpendicular (XL) to it. When the
nanotube composed of a mole number n of carbon atoms is placed in a
magnetic field //, the magnetic energy is given by
£(0,//) = -(n//-/2)[;^x+(/„-/i)cos^^] (1)
where 6 is the angle between the tube axis and field H {-nil < 6 < KI2).
The magnetic orientation takes place in the direction where the magnetic
energy E{0, H) is minimum. Since the nanotubes are oriented with the tube
axis parallel to the field {0 = 0), the susceptibilities are shown to hold the
relation Xi < Xw-

5.4.3 Magnetic Orientation and Thermal Disordering: Boltzmann
Distribution
Consider a molecule possessing an axis Z of magnetic symmetry and

--- Page 234 ---

5.4 Magnetic Alignment of Multi-wall Carbon Nanotubes 217
placed in a magnetic field H, The simplest case is one in which both the
axis Z and field H are restricted in a two-dimensional space. Since the axis
Z moves in the two-dimensional plane, the degree of freedom is 1 (one) for
the direction of the molecule, which is specified by the rotation angle 0
about the normal to that plane. (The magnetic energy E{e, H) is a
continuous function of the variable 6.) The directions of the molecules
follow the Boltzmann statistics at thermal equilibrium, and the probability
that the molecule exists between the angles 6 and ^ + d^ at temperature T is
written as
P(eM)de = cxp[-E(eM)/kT]de/ T' Q^p[-E(e.H)/kT]de (2)
J-7t/2
with k being the Boltzmann constant. The overall susceptibility anisotropy
n (Xw - Z-L) of the molecule is the only parameter involved in Eqs. (1) and
(2), and determined by simulation of the experimental results.
The physical meaning of the Boltzmann distribution for the
directions of the molecules is explained as follows. While the magnetic
field intensity is weak, the difference in the magnetic energy E(0, H) - £(0,
H) is small between any direction {0) and the magnetically stable direction
{e - 0). The distribution is disordered to be random by the thermal energy,
and has a wide width. The molecules exist in magnetically unstable
directions by the thermal motion and are not oriented. As the magnetic
field intensity grows stronger, the difference in the magnetic energy
becomes larger. The distribudon has a higher probability and narrower
width near the magnetically stable direction. When the difference in the
magnetic energy "exceeds" the thermal energy, the molecules are oriented
in the magnetically stable direction against the thermal disordering.
The reasonable approximation is that the carbon nanotubes lie in the
horizontal plane and are affected by the horizontal magnetic fields.
Calculation can be made to simulate the observed results for the
distribution of the directions of the nanotubes using Eqs. (1) and (2) as
functions of the variables 0 and H. The calculated results are also shown in
Fig. 5-4-2. The calculation reproduces the observation. Thus, the magnetic
orientation of the nanotubes is explained quantitatively by the Boltzmann
distribution for variation of the magnetic field intensity.
5.4.4 Estimation of Magnetic Anisotropy by Magnetic Orientation
The magnetic susceptibility anisotropy of the carbon nanotubes is assumed
to be independent of the field intensity (5.0-80.0 kOe). The susceptibility
anisotropy is estimated by simulation of the experimental results as'^
;f II - ;f 1 = (9 ± 5) X10~^ cm"^ moP' (per mole of carbon atoms)
The anisotropic susceptibilities have been reported for aligned carbon
nanotubes.^'^^ The alignment was made by production of bundles,"^ ^^ by
mechanical treatment (drawing of films*^^ and straining of composites^^).

With respect to the anisotropy of susceptibilities, a positive sign (xw -
